Direction and Gestures for the Rider in Prayer
Rulings on qibla direction and motions for a rider in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings for a rider's prayer (direction and gestures) and apply them to determine validity.

Core Idea
Rider prays in the animal's direction; turning away invalidates. Gestures replace full movements, with sujood lower.

The rider on a mount has a dispensation in two aspects of prayer: direction and physical movements. This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayirah clarifies that the rider must follow the animal's facing direction and cannot deliberately deviate without necessity. Further, instead of full bowing and prostration, the rider indicates them by gestures, with the gesture for prostration lower than that for bowing. Understanding these concessions and their limits is essential for anyone travelling and praying on a mount.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and extract the two distinct legal points: one about qibla direction, the other about body movements (ruku/sujood).
2
Analyze the direction ruling
The rider must pray in the direction the animal faces. Deliberately turning away to another direction renders the prayer invalid (la yajuz) because there is no necessity (la darurah). Understand the condition and its consequence.
This applies to voluntary prayers on a mount; for obligatory prayers, see other sources.
3
Analyze the gesture ruling
The rider performs ima' (gestures) for ruku and sujood, and must make the gesture for sujood lower than that for ruku, mimicking the natural posture hierarchy.
Ima' is done with the head/upper body; sujood should be more pronounced.
4
Connect the rulings to conditions and validity
Both rulings are conditions for the prayer to be valid. If the rider deliberately faces away from the animal's direction, the prayer is null. If the gestures are equal (e.g., same height) or inverted, the prayer may be defective.
The text only mentions invalidity for direction violation; for gestures, it is a requirement to differentiate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : إلى أي جهة توجهت به فإن صلى إلى غير ما توجهت به الدابة : لا يجوز؛ لعدم الضرورة. كذا في «الفتاوى».
- وقوله : يومىء إيماء: ويجعل السجود أخفض من الركوع.
His saying: In whatever direction it (the animal) faces, if he prays to other than the direction the animal faces: it is not valid, due to lack of necessity. Thus in al-Fatawa. And his saying: He gestures (ima'), and makes the prostration lower than the bowing.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This short passage gives two rulings for a person praying while riding. First, concerning direction (qibla): the rider prays in the direction the animal faces. If he intentionally turns his face to another direction while the animal is facing a different way, the prayer is invalid because there is no necessity (darurah) to do so. Second, concerning the physical movements: the rider performs ima' (gestures) instead of full ruku and sujood, and he makes the gesture for sujood lower than that for ruku, to indicate the proper posture hierarchy. This is the standard Hanafi position for the traveller praying on a mount in voluntary prayers.

Key Terms
إيماء (īmā'): Gestural indication with the head/body in place of full bowing and prostration
دابة (dābba): Riding animal (horse, camel, etc.)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a rider is praying on an animal that faces east, and he deliberately faces north, what is the ruling?
A) Valid, as long as he gestures correctly
B) Invalid, due to lack of necessity
C) Valid if he intends the general direction of qibla
D) Makruh but valid

Key Takeaway
The rider must pray in the animal's direction and gesture with sujood lower than ruku; deliberate deviation invalidates the prayer.

Prohibition for Pedestrian and Swimmer to Pray in Any Direction
Ruling with qiyas analogy to speaking, eating, drinking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a pedestrian or swimmer praying while moving and explain why it is invalid by citing the qiyas analogy to speaking, eating, and drinking.

Core Idea
A pedestrian cannot pray while walking because his own movement contradicts the stillness of prayer—unlike a rider carried by his mount.

You already know that performing extra actions during prayer can invalidate it. This passage extends that principle to a very specific case: praying while walking or swimming. The author uses a powerful qiyas (analogical reasoning) to show that self-initiated movement invalidates prayer just as clearly as speaking or eating.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State that the passage declares praying while walking (or swimming) as impermissible and invalid. Note the consensus: 'according to all of them'.
The ruling is general—applies to both obligatory and voluntary prayers.
2
Analyze the Qiyas Proof
The text gives the reason: 'because he is doing something that contradicts the prayer by his own action.' This is compared to speaking, eating, and drinking, which are undisputed invalidators. Identify the common illa (effective cause): an action that directly opposes the nature of prayer (stillness, focus).
The qiyas is jali (clear) because the illa is obvious and explicitly stated.
3
Distinguish from the Rider Exemption
Recall that a rider may perform voluntary prayer while the mount moves, because the movement is not generated by him. The pedestrian (or swimmer) moves by his own exertion, so the exemption does not apply. This contrast clarifies the illa.
If you ride today (e.g., in a car), the same principle may apply—stay tuned for modern applications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز للماشي أن يصلي أين كان وجهه عندهم جميعاً؛ لأنه فاعل لما ينافي الصلاة بنفسه، فصار كالكلام والأكل والشرب.
وكذا لا يجوز في حالة السباحة؛ لأنه كالمشي.
And it is not permissible for the pedestrian to pray in whatever direction his face is, according to all of them; because he is doing something that contradicts the prayer by his own action, so it becomes like speaking, eating, and drinking. Similarly, it is not permissible in the state of swimming, because it is like walking.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage extends the ruling of the rider to the pedestrian and swimmer. A pedestrian cannot pray in any direction he walks, because walking itself is an action that directly contradicts the stillness required in prayer. The text analogizes it to speaking, eating, and drinking – all invalidators of prayer. Similarly, swimming is also prohibited because it involves movement of the body contrary to prayer. Unlike the rider, who is carried by the animal and thus excused in voluntary prayer, the pedestrian moves by his own volition and exertion, so the exemption does not apply.

Key Terms
ماشي (māshī): A pedestrian, one walking on foot
سباحة (sibāḥah): Swimming
ينافي الصلاة (yunāfī al-ṣalāt): Contradicts the nature/purpose of prayer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is praying while walking invalid according to the text?
A) Because the person is moving, which is makruh
B) Because the person is doing an act that contradicts prayer, like speaking
C) Because it is difficult to maintain wudu
D) Because the direction cannot be determined

Key Takeaway
A pedestrian or swimmer cannot pray while moving because their self-initiated movement is an act that contradicts the essence of prayer, analogous to speaking, eating, or drinking.

Impurity on the Saddle in Prayer: Zahir al-Riwaya vs. Later Fatwa
Dispute between Zahir al-Riwayah and later Fatawa on excusing impurity on saddle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on impurity on the saddle and distinguish between the Zahir al-Riwaya position and that of Muhammad ibn Muqatil.

Core Idea
Zahir al-Riwaya excuses any impurity on the saddle; later scholars differentiate by type—know which is mufta bihi.

When praying on a riding animal, impurity on the saddle raises a question. The Hanafi school's primary transmission (zahir al-riwaya) takes a lenient view, excusing any impurity regardless of type. However, some later scholars, like Muhammad ibn Muqatil, distinguish between different impurities. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Zahir al-Riwaya)
What is the ruling according to the established transmission? Does it differentiate between types of impurity?
Look for the phrase 'لا بأس به' and 'على ظاهر الرواية'.
2
Identify the opposing view
Muhammad ibn Muqatil holds a different view. What distinction does he make? Which impurities are excused and which are not?
The text mentions 'إذا كان دماً أو عذرة أو بولاً لم يجز'.
3
Analyze the basis of each position
Why does Zahir al-Riwaya not differentiate? Why does Ibn Muqatil differentiate? The commentary provides reasoning.
Zahir al-Riwaya is built on leniency (takhfif) and analogy to impurity on the animal's body.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
How does the ruling differ in a real scenario? For example, blood on the saddle vs. donkey saliva.
Under Zahir al-Riwaya, both are excused; under Ibn Muqatil, only donkey saliva is excused.
5
Tarjih: which position is relied upon?
The text indicates that the relied position is the Zahir al-Riwaya. Why is it preferred?
The phrase 'على ظاهر الرواية' suggests it is the primary authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان على سرج الدابة نجاسة أكثر من قدر الدرهم: لا بأس به، على ظاهر الرواية.
قال في «الفتاوى»: يعني إذا كان من لعاب الحمار، أما إذا كان دماً، أو عذرة، أو بولاً : لم يجز، وهو قول محمد بن مقاتل.
وأما في ظاهر الرواية : لم يفصل بينهما، وجوز ذلك؛ لأن بناه على التخفيف.
وفي «شرحه»: لا تفسد صلاته ؛ لأنه غير متصرف في السرج، فأشبه ما إذا كان على الدابة نجاسة، فإنه لا يُؤمر بغسلها، كذلك هذا.
And if there is impurity on the saddle of the riding animal more than the size of a dirham: there is no harm in it, according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). It is said in al-Fatawa: meaning if it is from donkey saliva; as for if it is blood, feces, or urine: it is not permissible, and this is the saying of Muhammad ibn Muqatil. As for the apparent narration: it did not differentiate between them, and permitted it because it is built on leniency. And in its commentary: It does not invalidate his prayer because he is not acting upon the saddle, so it resembles when there is impurity on the animal itself, for which he is not commanded to wash it; likewise this.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage deals with impurity (najasa) on the saddle (sari) of the animal while praying on it. According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) of the Hanafi school, even if the impurity exceeds the size of a dirham, it is excused (la ba'sa bihi) because the ruling is built on leniency (takhfif). However, in al-Fatawa, a distinction is reported: if the impurity is from donkey saliva (lu'ab al-himar), it is excused; if it is blood (dam), feces (adhra), or urine (bawl), it is not permissible. This is the position of Muhammad ibn Muqatil. The commentary (sharh) explains why: the prayer is not invalidated because the person is not directly acting upon the saddle (ghayr mutasarrif fi al-sarij); similarly, impurity on the animal itself is not required to be washed for the validity of prayer. This shows a tension between the more lenient established narration and a later more detailed fatwa.

Key Terms
سرج (sarj): The saddle of a riding animal
نجاسة (najāsah): Ritual impurity
ظاهر الرواية (zāhir al-riwāyah): The established and transmitted narration of the Hanafi school (from the books of Imam Muhammad)
لعاب الحمار (lu‘āb al-ḥimār): Saliva of a donkey

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zahir al-Riwaya, what is the ruling if impurity (e.g., blood) exceeding a dirham is on the saddle?
A) Prayer is invalid
B) It is excused, prayer valid
C) It is excused only if the impurity is from donkey saliva
D) It is makruh but valid

Key Takeaway
The established Hanafi position (zahir al-riwaya) does not differentiate between types of impurity on the saddle; later fatwas distinguishing based on type are not the primary authority.

Ruling on Walking and Swimming During Prayer
Ruling on movement during prayer with qiyas proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why walking and swimming nullify prayer in the Hanafi school by applying qiyas to established nullifiers.

Core Idea
Walking and swimming contradict the essence of prayer, just like speaking, eating, or drinking.

The passage begins by addressing a common scenario: if a person is walking and wants to pray, they cannot simply pray in any direction. The Hanafi school rules that such movement invalidates the prayer, drawing an analogy to well-known nullifiers. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and extends it to swimming.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is praying while walking. The ruling is not permissible (la yajuz).
The text says 'عندهم جميعاً' indicating unanimity within the school.
2
Locate the asl (original case)
The established nullifiers of prayer: speaking, eating, drinking. These are known to invalidate prayer based on hadith.
The text directly compares: 'فصار كالكلام والأكل والشرب'.
3
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that these actions inherently contradict the nature of prayer (منافي للصلاة بنفسه).
The illa is clear: any action that goes against the essence of prayer.
4
Apply qiyas to the far' (new case)
Walking is an action that contradicts the nature of prayer, therefore it also nullifies prayer.
The analogy is straightforward (qiyas jali).
5
Extend to swimming
Swimming involves even more movement and is also an action that contradicts the essence of prayer; it is directly analogous (كالمشي).
The text says 'وكذا لا يجوز في حالة السباحة؛ لأنه كالمشي'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب النوافل
ولا يجوز للماشي أن يصلي أين كان وجهه عندهم جميعاً؛ لأنه فاعل لما ينافي الصلاة بنفسه، فصار كالكلام والأكل والشرب.
وكذا لا يجوز في حالة السباحة؛ لأنه كالمشي.
وإذا كان على سرج الدابة نجاسة أكثر من قدر الدرهم: لا بأس به، على ظاهر الرواية.
قال في «الفتاوى»: يعني إذا كان من لعاب الحمار، أما إذا كان دماً، أو عذرة، أو بولاً : لم يجز، وهو قول محمد بن مقاتل.
وأما في ظاهر الرواية : لم يفصل بينهما، وجوز ذلك؛ لأن بناه على التخفيف.
وفي «شرحه»: لا تفسد صلاته ؛ لأنه غير متصرف في السرج، فأشبه ما إذا كان على الدابة نجاسة، فإنه لا يُؤمر بغسلها، كذلك هذا.
Chapter of supererogatory prayers. It is not permissible for the one walking to pray wherever his face is directed, according to all of them, because he is doing something that contradicts prayer in itself, so it becomes like speaking, eating, and drinking. Likewise, it is not permissible while swimming, because it is like walking. If there is impurity on the saddle of the riding animal more than the size of a dirham, it is not a problem, according to the apparent transmission. He said in al-Fatawa: meaning if it is from the saliva of a donkey; but if it is blood, feces, or urine, it is not permissible, and this is the statement of Muhammad ibn Muqatil. As for the apparent transmission, it did not differentiate between them and permitted it, because it (the saddle) is built on leniency. And in its commentary: His prayer is not invalidated because he is not acting upon the saddle, so it resembles the case where there is impurity on the animal itself, for which he is not ordered to wash it; likewise this.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on rulings related to prayer and movement. First, it states a clear ruling: a person walking cannot pray in any direction because walking itself is an action that contradicts the essence of prayer; it is analogized to speaking, eating, and drinking, which are known nullifiers. Then it extends the same ruling to swimming, as it involves similar movement. Next, it addresses impurity on the saddle of a riding animal. According to the well-known transmission (zahir al-riwayah), if the impurity exceeds the size of a dirham, it is still excused because the saddle is built on leniency. However, a later fatwa from Muhammad ibn Muqatil differentiates: if the impurity is from donkey saliva, it is permissible; if it is blood, feces, or urine, it is not. The text notes that the apparent transmission does not differentiate and permits all, but the commentary explains that the prayer is not invalidated because the person is not directly handling the saddle; it is akin to impurity on the animal itself, which does not require immediate washing during prayer. The Hanafi position is that such impurity does not invalidate the prayer, with a nuanced ikhtilaf on the type of impurity.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-established transmitted rulings from the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered the most authoritative source in the Hanafi school.
التخفيف (al-takhfif): The principle of leniency applied to matters of prayer to avoid hardship, used here to justify excusing impurity on the saddle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the ruling that walking nullifies prayer?
A) Direct hadith
B) Qiyas to speaking
C) Ijma
D) Istihsan

Key Takeaway
Walking and swimming nullify prayer because they contradict its nature, just like speaking.

Ruling on Impurity on the Saddle During Prayer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on impurity on the saddle during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on impurity exceeding a dirham on the saddle and distinguish between the zahir al-riwayah and the opinion of Muhammad ibn Muqatil.

Core Idea
Impurity on the saddle, even if large, does not invalidate prayer according to the well-known Hanafi position.

This passage addresses a practical concern for those praying on horseback. The presence of impurity on the saddle raises questions about the validity of prayer. The Hanafi school holds a lenient view based on the principle of takhfif and the fact that the worshipper is not directly handling the saddle. However, an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf exists regarding the type of impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The issue is the presence of impurity (najasa) on the saddle of the riding animal, exceeding the size of a dirham.
The text specifies 'أكثر من قدر الدرهم'.
2
State the zahir al-riwayah position
According to the well-known transmission, it does not prevent the validity of prayer.
The text says 'لا بأس به'.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Muhammad ibn Muqatil differentiates: if the impurity is from donkey saliva, it is excused; if blood, feces, or urine, it invalidates.
This is a later view recorded in al-Fatawa.
4
Analyze the basis for leniency
The reason for leniency is that the saddle is built on takhfif (ease) and the worshipper is not directly acting upon the saddle.
The commentary says 'غير متصرف في السرج' and analogizes to impurity on the animal itself.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the zahir al-riwayah: impurity on the saddle does not invalidate prayer regardless of type.
The text indicates that the apparent transmission is the default.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب النوافل
ولا يجوز للماشي أن يصلي أين كان وجهه عندهم جميعاً؛ لأنه فاعل لما ينافي الصلاة بنفسه، فصار كالكلام والأكل والشرب.
وكذا لا يجوز في حالة السباحة؛ لأنه كالمشي.
وإذا كان على سرج الدابة نجاسة أكثر من قدر الدرهم: لا بأس به، على ظاهر الرواية.
قال في «الفتاوى»: يعني إذا كان من لعاب الحمار، أما إذا كان دماً، أو عذرة، أو بولاً : لم يجز، وهو قول محمد بن مقاتل.
وأما في ظاهر الرواية : لم يفصل بينهما، وجوز ذلك؛ لأن بناه على التخفيف.
وفي «شرحه»: لا تفسد صلاته ؛ لأنه غير متصرف في السرج، فأشبه ما إذا كان على الدابة نجاسة، فإنه لا يُؤمر بغسلها، كذلك هذا.
Chapter of supererogatory prayers. It is not permissible for the one walking to pray wherever his face is directed, according to all of them, because he is doing something that contradicts prayer in itself, so it becomes like speaking, eating, and drinking. Likewise, it is not permissible while swimming, because it is like walking. If there is impurity on the saddle of the riding animal more than the size of a dirham, it is not a problem, according to the apparent transmission. He said in al-Fatawa: meaning if it is from the saliva of a donkey; but if it is blood, feces, or urine, it is not permissible, and this is the statement of Muhammad ibn Muqatil. As for the apparent transmission, it did not differentiate between them and permitted it, because it (the saddle) is built on leniency. And in its commentary: His prayer is not invalidated because he is not acting upon the saddle, so it resembles the case where there is impurity on the animal itself, for which he is not ordered to wash it; likewise this.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on rulings related to prayer and movement. First, it states a clear ruling: a person walking cannot pray in any direction because walking itself is an action that contradicts the essence of prayer; it is analogized to speaking, eating, and drinking, which are known nullifiers. Then it extends the same ruling to swimming, as it involves similar movement. Next, it addresses impurity on the saddle of a riding animal. According to the well-known transmission (zahir al-riwayah), if the impurity exceeds the size of a dirham, it is still excused because the saddle is built on leniency. However, a later fatwa from Muhammad ibn Muqatil differentiates: if the impurity is from donkey saliva, it is permissible; if it is blood, feces, or urine, it is not. The text notes that the apparent transmission does not differentiate and permits all, but the commentary explains that the prayer is not invalidated because the person is not directly handling the saddle; it is akin to impurity on the animal itself, which does not require immediate washing during prayer. The Hanafi position is that such impurity does not invalidate the prayer, with a nuanced ikhtilaf on the type of impurity.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-established transmitted rulings from the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered the most authoritative source in the Hanafi school.
التخفيف (al-takhfif): The principle of leniency applied to matters of prayer to avoid hardship, used here to justify excusing impurity on the saddle.
نجاسة (najasa): Impurity, ritual filth that can affect the validity of prayer if present on the person or clothing beyond a certain amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Invalidates prayer
B) Does not invalidate prayer
C) Only invalidates if intentional
D) Depends on the amount

Key Takeaway
Impurity on the saddle, regardless of type, does not invalidate prayer according to the well-known Hanafi transmission.

Sajdah al-Sahw: Obligatory After Taslim for Increase and Decrease
Introducing the chapter on sajdah al-sahw and stating its ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sajdah al-sahw, identify its triggers (increase and decrease due to forgetfulness), and explain that it is performed after the taslim.

Core Idea
Sajdah al-sahw is wajib after taslim for any forgetful increase or decrease.

This lesson opens the chapter on sajdah al-sahw, the prostration of forgetfulness. The commentator explains why it comes after the discussion of supererogatory prayers—because it compensates for deficiencies in all prayers. We will examine the ruling itself, its timing, and the key terms sahw and nisyan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the ruling
The passage begins with 'باب سجود السهو' and states 'سجود السهو واجب في الزيادة والنقصان بعد السلام'. Determine what is being ruled upon (sajdah al-sahw) and what the ruling is (واجب = wajib).
Wajib in Hanafi fiqh is a legal obligation, less than fard but still binding.
2
Determine the timing and triggers
The ruling specifies that sajdah al-sahw is performed بعد السلام (after the taslim) and it applies to both الزيادة (increase) and النقصان (decrease) in the prayer. Understand that it covers all forgetfulness-related errors.
This differs from other schools that may require it before taslim for deficiencies.
3
Analyze the author's placement logic
The commentator explains why this chapter is placed after the discussion of supererogatory prayers: sajdah al-sahw compensates deficiencies in both obligatory and supererogatory prayers, similar to how supererogatory prayers compensate deficiencies in obligatory ones. Reflect on the structure of the fiqh text.
This shows the systematic organization of Islamic jurisprudence.
4
Distinguish between sahw and nisyan
The text defines nisyan as the departure of something from the mind after it was present, while sahw can occur even in matters one never knew. Recognize that sahw is the broader term that triggers the prostration, not only nisyan.
This linguistic distinction has legal implications: any inadvertence (sahw) in prayer may necessitate sajdah al-sahw, not just forgetting something previously known.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a scenario where a worshipper accidentally adds an extra rakat or omits a wajib element. According to this passage, sajdah al-sahw becomes wajib after taslim. Verify that both increase and decrease are covered.
Note: the prayer must still be valid overall; deliberate changes would invalidate the prayer and require full repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب سجود السهو
باب سجود السهو
سجود السهو واجب في الزيادة والنقصان بعد السلام،
لما انتهى ذكرُ الأداء من الفرائض والنوافل والقضاء، شَرَعَ في جَبْر نقصان يتمكن فيهما جميعاً.
في كما ذَكَرَ النوافل بعد أداء الفرائض ؛ لكونها جبراً لنقصان تمكّن الفرائض، فلهذا ذَكَرَ السهو عقيب النوافل ؛ لكونه جبراً للنقصان المتمكن في الأداء والقضاء، والفرائض والنوافل، فكان بعد الجميع.
وهو من باب إضافة الشيء إلى سببه.
والسهو والنسيان ضدُّ الذكر، إلا أن بين السهو والنسيان فرقاً، وهو: أن النسيان: عُزوب الشيء عن النفس بعد حضوره.
والسهو: قد يكون عما كان الإنسان به عالماً، وعما لا يكون عالماً به. قال رحمه الله: (سجود السهو واجب في الزيادة والنقصان) سواء، (بعد السلام).
Chapter of prostration of forgetfulness. Chapter of prostration of forgetfulness. The prostration of forgetfulness is obligatory for increase and decrease after the taslim. Since the discussion of the performance of obligatory, supererogatory, and make-up prayers has concluded, he begins on compensation for deficiency that occurs in both. Just as he mentioned the supererogatory prayers after the performance of obligatory prayers, because they are compensation for deficiency that occurs in obligatory prayers, so he mentioned forgetfulness after supererogatory prayers, because it is compensation for deficiency that occurs in performance and make-up, and in obligatory and supererogatory prayers, so it comes after all. And this is from the category of adding a thing to its cause. And forgetfulness (sahw) and forgetting (nisyan) are opposites of remembrance, but there is a difference between sahw and nisyan: nisyan is the departure of a thing from the mind after its presence; sahw can be regarding something that a person knew or did not know. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'The prostration of forgetfulness is obligatory for increase and decrease in all cases, after the taslim.'
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on sajdah al-sahw (prostration of forgetfulness). The commentator first explains the placement of this chapter: after discussing the performance of obligatory, supererogatory, and make-up prayers, the author now turns to the compensation for deficiencies that may occur in all these prayers. The reasoning is similar to why supererogatory prayers were mentioned after obligatory prayers — they serve as compensation for the deficiencies of the obligatory prayers. Likewise, sajdah al-sahw is placed after the discussion of supererogatory prayers because it compensates for forgetful errors in both obligatory and supererogatory prayers, and in both performance (ada') and make-up (qada'). The commentator then adds that this chapter is an example of 'adding a thing to its cause' — meaning that forgetfulness is the cause for this prostration. Next, a linguistic distinction is made: nisyan (forgetting) is the disappearance of something from the mind after it was present, while sahw (forgetfulness) can occur both in matters one knew and in matters one did not know. Finally, the ruling is stated: sajdah al-sahw is obligatory (wajib) for any increase or decrease in the prayer, and it is performed after the taslim (final greeting). This is a foundational ruling for the chapter.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (Sajdah al-Sahw): Two prostrations performed to compensate for forgetfulness in prayer, obligatory after the taslim.
السهو (al-sahw): Forgetfulness or inadvertence during prayer, which may lead to an increase or decrease in the prayer's actions. Distinguished from nisyan by being broader: sahw includes both what one knew and did not know.
النسيان (al-nisyan): Forgetting something after it was present in the mind, considered a subset of sahw.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Before taslim
B) After taslim
C) Only if increase occurred
D) Only if decrease occurred

Key Takeaway
Sajdah al-sahw is a wajib act performed after the taslim to compensate for any forgetful increase or decrease in prayer.

Timing of Sajdah al-Sahw: Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on timing of Sajdah al-Sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the timing of Sajdah al-Sahw and evaluate the Shafi'i and Maliki counterarguments, identifying the practical consequences of each view.

Core Idea
Sajdah al-Sahw is wajib after salam for both increase and decrease; before salam is valid but not optimal.

The chapter on Sajdah al-Sahw (prostration for forgetfulness) addresses a common yet crucial issue in prayer: how to compensate for unintentional changes. While all schools agree on the compensation itself, the timing—before or after the salam—sparks a well-known inter-madhhab dispute. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, the arguments of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik, and the important nuance that the disagreement is rooted in precedence (al-awlawiyyah) rather than validity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: Sajdah al-Sahw is wajib, performed after the salam for both increase and decrease in prayer. Note the sequence: two prostrations, tashahhud, two salams.
The text says 'بعد السلام' (after the salam) covering both types.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their views
Recognize that al-Shafi'i holds it before salam for both, while Malik distinguishes: before salam for decrease, after for increase.
This is a classic inter-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the Hanafi response to disagreement
The author reframes the dispute as one of priority (al-awlawiyyah) rather than validity. Performing before salam is permissible (ja'iz) but after is better (awla).
This demonstrates Hanafi methodology: validity is granted even when a weaker view is followed.
4
Analyze the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical outcome: a Hanafi worshipper will always perform sajdah after salam; a Shafi'i before; a Maliki depending on the cause. All prayers remain valid if the respective rulings are followed.
Cross-madhhab tolerance: the text does not declare other views invalid.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi variant on number of salams
Within the Hanafi school, the correct view is two salams (taslimatayn), though Fakhr al-Islam held one salam suffices while facing the qiblah. The text affirms 'huwa al-sahih' (it is the correct).
This shows that even within the school there is minor variation, but the mufta bihi is two salams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب سجود السهو
سجود السهو واجب في الزيادة والنقصان بعد السلام،
يسجد سجدتين، ثم يتشهد، ويسلم.
وقال الشافعي: قبل السلام فيهما.
وقال مالك: إن كان للنقصان فقبل السلام، وإن كان للزيادة: فبعد السلام.
والخلاف في الأولوية، حتى لو سجد عندنا قبل السلام: جاز، إلا أن الأول أولى.
* قوله: (يسجد سجدتين، ثم يتشهد، ويسلم).
فيه إشارة إلى أن سجود السهو يرفع التشهد والسلام، ولكن لا يرفع القعدة؛ لأن الأقوى لا يرتفع بالأدنى، بخلاف السجدة الصلبية؛ لأنها أقوى من القعدة، فترفعها.
وقوله: ويسلّم: أي يأتي بالتسليمتين، هو الصحيح.
وقال فخر الإسلام: يسلم تسليمةً واحدةً تلقاء وجهه، ولا ينحرف عن القبلة، وهذا خلاف المشهور.
ومن عليه سجدتا السهو في الفجر إذا لم يسجد حتى طلعت الشمس بعد ما قعد قدر التشهد: سقطتا عنه.
وكذا إذا سها في قضاء الفائتة، فلم يسجد حتى احمرت الشمس.
Chapter of Sajdah al-Sahw. Sajdah al-Sahw is obligatory for increase and decrease after the salam. He prostrates two prostrations, then recites tashahhud, and makes salam. Al-Shafi'i said: before salam in both cases. Malik said: if it is for decrease then before salam, and if for increase then after salam. The difference is in precedence, such that if he prostrated before salam according to us, it is permissible, but the first is better. * His statement: 'He prostrates two prostrations, then recites tashahhud, and makes salam.' In it is an indication that sajdah al-sahw raises the tashahhud and salam, but it does not raise the sitting; because the stronger is not raised by the weaker, unlike the prostrations of the prayer itself because they are stronger than the sitting, so they raise it. His statement: 'and makes salam' means that he performs two salams; this is the correct view. Fakhr al-Islam said: he makes one salam facing his direction without turning from the qiblah; this is contrary to the well-known view. For one upon whom sajdah al-sahw is due in Fajr, if he does not prostrate until the sun rises after having sat for the amount of tashahhud, it is dropped. Likewise, if he forgets in making up a missed prayer and does not prostrate until the sun becomes red.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Sajdah al-Sahw (prostration for forgetfulness), which serves to compensate for deficiencies in prayer. The author begins by stating the Hanafi ruling: Sajdah al-Sahw is obligatory (wajib) for both increase and decrease in prayer, and it is performed after the salam. The sequence is: two prostrations, then tashahhud, then salam. The author then presents the disagreement with al-Shafi'i, who holds that it should be performed before the salam for both types, and with Malik, who distinguishes: if the forgetfulness involved a decrease, it is before salam; if an increase, after salam. However, the author clarifies that according to the Hanafi school, the difference is only in priority (al-awlawiyyah): if one performs it before salam, it is still valid, but performing it after salam is better. The commentary then analyzes the phrase 'then he prostrates, then recites tashahhud, and makes salam.' It explains that Sajdah al-Sahw supersedes (rafa'a) the requirement of tashahhud and salam that would normally follow the prostrations? Actually, the text says it 'raises' (yarfa') the tashahhud and salam, meaning they are repeated after the sajdah? The commentary clarifies that Sajdah al-Sahw raises (i.e., cancels the previous tashahhud and salam and requires new ones) but does not raise the sitting (qa'dah) because the stronger (the sitting with tashahhud) is not raised by the weaker (Sajdah al-Sahw). In contrast, the prostrations of the prayer itself (sajdah sulbiyyah) are stronger than the sitting, so they raise it. Regarding the salam, the correct view is that one performs two salams (taslimatayn), though Fakhr al-Islam held that one salam is sufficient while facing the qiblah without turning. The passage concludes with two exceptions: if a person owes Sajdah al-Sahw in Fajr prayer and does not perform it until after sunrise, having already sat for the duration of tashahhud, then the sajdah is dropped. Similarly, if one forgets in making up a missed prayer and does not prostrate until the sun becomes red (i.e., the disliked time), the sajdah is dropped.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sajdah al-sahw): Prostration performed to compensate for forgetfulness (increase or decrease) in prayer, consisting of two sajdahs
الزيادة والنقصان (al-ziyādah wa al-nuqṣān): Increase (e.g., adding an extra rakat or action) or decrease (e.g., omitting a wajib or sunnah) in the prayer due to forgetfulness
القعدة (al-qa'dah): The sitting for tashahhud in the prayer; it is a wajib according to Hanafis, but not a rukn
التسليمة (al-taslīmah): The salam (greeting of peace) at the end of prayer; two taslimahs are standard in Hanafi

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when should one perform Sajdah al-Sahw?
A. Before the salam for both increase and decrease
B. After the salam for both increase and decrease
C. Before the salam for decrease, after for increase
D. Only if the prayer is a missed prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling is that Sajdah al-Sahw is performed after salam for both increase and decrease, though before salam is permissible but not optimal.

Timing of Sajdah al-Sahw: Preference vs Validity
Inter-madhhab dispute on timing of sajdah al-sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the timing of sajdah al-sahw, compare it with the Shafi'i and Maliki positions, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for a worshipper.

Core Idea
Sajdah al-sahw may be performed before or after salam in the Hanafi school, but after salam is better — the dispute is about preference, not validity.

This passage from the chapter on sajdah al-sahw presents the Hanafi ruling on the timing of the prostration of forgetfulness, then contrasts it with the views of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik. The key insight is that the disagreement among the schools is a matter of priority (awlawiyyah), not of validity. This means a Hanafi follower has flexibility while being guided toward the optimal practice — a lesson in understanding how fiqh distinguishes between what is obligatory and what is merely better.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify from the matn and commentary that the Hanafi default sequence is: two sajdahs, then tashahhud, then salam. The commentary clarifies that after salam is 'first' (avval) or 'better' (awla), but before is permissible (ja'iz).
This is the mufta bihi: preferred after salam, but both are valid.
2
Identify the opposing views
Extract the Shafi'i position: sajdah al-sahw must be before salam in every case. Extract the Maliki position: if due to deficiency, before salam; if due to addition, after salam.
These views are presented as direct quotes (Qala al-Shafi'i, Qala Malik).
3
Analyze the nature of the khilaf
The text explicitly says 'الخلاف في الأولوية' — the disagreement is about preference (awlawiyyah), not about validity (sihhah). This means all positions are valid; the difference is which timing is considered more virtuous or cautious.
Understanding 'awlawiyyah' is key: it indicates a range of valid practice.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi may choose either timing without invalidating the prayer; a Shafi'i must do before salam; a Maliki must distinguish by cause. Flexibility vs. strictness.
Contrast: if a Hanafi intentionally does before, it's valid but makruh tanzihan? Not specified here; simply 'less preferred'.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The text declares after salam as 'اول' (first/preferred). This is the established position in Zahir al-Riwayah. The door is left open for permissibility of before salam to accommodate forgetfulness or other factors.
Later Hanafi works (e.g., al-Hidayah) may elaborate further, but the core principle remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب سجود السهو يسجد سجدتين، ثم يتشهد، ويسلم. (1) وقال الشافعي: قبل السلام فيهما. وقال مالك (۲) : إن كان للنقصان فقبل السلام، وإن كان للزيادة: فبعد السلام. والخلاف في الأولوية، حتى لو سجد عندنا قبل السلام: جاز، إلا أن الأول أولى.
Chapter of Prostration of Forgetfulness. He prostrates two prostrations, then recites the tashahhud, and says the salam. (1) Al-Shafi'i said: Before the salam in both. (2) Malik said: If it is for a deficiency, then before the salam; if it is for an addition, then after the salam. The disagreement is regarding preference, such that if he prostrates before the salam according to us, it is permissible, but the first is better.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage presents the chapter on sajdah al-sahw. The matn states the basic order: two sajdahs, then tashahhud, then salam. The commentary then presents the inter-madhhab dispute. Al-Shafi'i holds that both sajdahs should be before salam. Malik differentiates: if for a deficiency, before salam; if for an addition, after. The Hanafi position is that performing sajdah after salam is preferable (أولى), but performing it before is also permissible (جاز). The khilaf is about priority, not validity. This reflects the Hanafi approach: flexibility with recommended practice.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sajdah al-sahw): Prostration performed to compensate for forgetfulness in prayer, consisting of two sajdahs.
أولوية (awlawiyyah): Preference or superiority of one valid option over another, not affecting validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The sajdah is not accepted.

Key Takeaway
The timing of sajdah al-sahw is flexible in the Hanafi school; after salam is preferred but before salam is permissible, reflecting the school's emphasis on ease and valid difference of opinion.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Effect of Sajdah al-Sahw on Tashahhud and Salam
Expands matn on order of sajdah al-sahw with talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why sajdah al-sahw does not cancel the final sitting (qa'dah), distinguish it from sajdah salbiyyah, and state the mufta bihi position on the number of taslims after sajdah al-sahw.

Core Idea
The stronger is not raised by the weaker: sajdah al-sahw postpones tashahhud and salam but not the final sitting.

This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah clarifies a key detail: when sajdah al-sahw is required, it does not negate the fundamental sitting posture. Instead, it only supersedes the tashahhud and salam that follow. By comparing it with sajdah salbiyyah, the passage reveals a principle of relative strength that governs the sequence of prayer acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on Order
The matn says: 'He prostrates two prostrations, then recites tashahhud, and says salam.' This places sajdah al-sahw between the final qa'dah and the tashahhud/salam.
The final qa'dah is performed before sajdah al-sahw.
2
Understand 'Raf' (Raising)
The commentary says sajdah al-sahw 'raises' the tashahhud and salam – meaning it cancels and replaces them such that they must be repeated after the sajdah.
It does not mean the acts are eliminated; they are postponed.
3
Distinguish the Two Sajdahs
Sajdah salbiyyah (tilawah prostration) is stronger than qa'dah, so it cancels the qa'dah. Sajdah al-sahw is weaker, so it cannot cancel the qa'dah.
The illa (cause) is the relative strength (quwwah).
4
Apply the Principle
The commentary states: 'لأن الأقوى لا يرتفع بالأدنى' – the stronger (qa'dah) is not raised by the weaker (sajdah al-sahw). This is the core legal reasoning.
This principle applies to all similar comparisons.
5
Resolve the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
The correct view (is al-sahih) is two taslims after sajdah al-sahw. Fakhr al-Islam's view of one taslim is against the well-known (mashhur).
The mufta bihi is two taslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله: (يسجد سجدتين، ثم يتشهد، ويسلم). فيه إشارة إلى أن سجود السهو يرفع التشهد والسلام، ولكن لا يرفع القعدة؛ لأن الأقوى لا يرتفع بالأدنى، بخلاف السجدة الصلبية؛ لأنها أقوى من القعدة، فترفعها. وقوله : ويسلّم : أي يأتي بالتسليمتين، هو الصحيح. وقال فخر الإسلام: يسلم تسليمةً واحدةً تلقاء وجهه، ولا ينحرف عن القبلة، وهذا خلاف المشهور.
* His words: (He prostrates two prostrations, then recites tashahhud, and says salam). In this there is an indication that sajdah al-sahw raises the tashahhud and salam, but it does not raise the sitting because the stronger is not raised by the weaker, contrary to the bodily prostration because it is stronger than the sitting, so it raises it. And his words: 'and says salam' means he performs two taslims; this is the correct view. And Fakhr al-Islam said: he says one taslim facing his face, and does not turn away from the qibla, and this is contrary to the well-known.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn, explaining the legal effect of sajdah al-sahw on the subsequent actions. It clarifies that sajdah al-sahw 'raises' (i.e., supersedes) the tashahhud and salam that follow it, meaning they are performed after the sajdah. However, it does not raise the qa'dah (the sitting before the tashahhud) because the qa'dah is a stronger act (a pillar) and the weaker (sajdah al-sahw) cannot cancel the stronger. This is contrasted with the prostration of recitation (sajdah salbiyyah), which is stronger than the qa'dah and thus does raise it. The commentary also addresses the number of taslims: the correct view is two taslims; Fakhr al-Islam's view of one taslim is against the mashhur.

Key Terms
القعدة (al-qa'dah): The sitting posture in prayer, specifically the final sitting for tashahhud.
السجدة الصلبية (al-sajdah al-salbiyyah): The prostration of recitation (tilawah) performed during prayer upon reciting a verse of prostration.
تسليمة (taslimah): The utterance of 'al-salamu alaykum' to exit prayer; two taslims are usual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does sajdah al-sahw not raise the qa'dah?
A) Because the qa'dah is performed before sajdah al-sahw.
B) Because sajdah al-sahw is stronger than the qa'dah.
C) Because the stronger is not raised by the weaker.
D) Because the qa'dah is a sunnah, not a fard.

Key Takeaway
Sajdah al-sahw raises tashahhud and salam but not the final sitting, because the weaker does not supersede the stronger.

Sajdah al-Sahw Dropping Due to Time Expiry (Fajr, Qada, Jumu'ah)
Rulings on sajdah al-sahw dropped when time expires

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases in which sajdah al-sahw is waived due to time expiry and explain the underlying principle.

Core Idea
If the prayer's time ends before you perform sajdah al-sahw, the sajdah is dropped — it cannot be made up later.

Sajdah al-sahw is normally performed within the prayer to correct forgetfulness. But what if the prayer's time expires before you do it? This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah addresses three specific scenarios: Fajr after sunrise, a makeup prayer (qada) after the sun becomes red, and Jumu'ah after its time. Understanding these exceptions prevents unnecessary repetition or invalidating the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Sajdah al-sahw is wajib when a forgetfulness error occurs. The ruling here is that it is dropped (waived) if the time for that prayer ends.
The Arabic verb 'سقطتا' indicates they fall away completely.
2
Note the three specific cases
The passage lists: (1) Fajr prayer – if sunrise occurs after one has sat for tashahhud; (2) Qada (makeup) of a missed prayer – if the sun becomes red (end of 'Asr time); (3) Jumu'ah – if its time exits.
These are exceptions to the rule that sajdah al-sahw must be performed before salam.
3
Extract the underlying principle
Sajdah al-sahw is part of the prayer and cannot be performed outside the prayer's valid time. If the time expires, the sajdah is no longer valid, so it is dropped without penalty.
This principle ensures the prayer remains valid despite the omission.
4
Recognize the authority citation
The passage ends with 'كذا في الفتاوى' – 'As in al-Fatawa.' This indicates the ruling is from later authoritative fatwa collections, showing it is the relied-upon position.
Al-Fatawa likely refers to a collection like Al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عليه سجدتا السهو في الفجر إذا لم يسجد حتى طلعت الشمس بعد ما قعد قدر التشهد : سقطتا عنه. وكذا إذا سها في قضاء الفائتة، فلم يسجد حتى احمرت الشمس. وفي الجمعة : إذا خرج وقتها. كذا في «الفتاوى».
And whoever owes the two sajdahs of forgetfulness in Fajr, if he does not prostrate until the sun rises after he has sat for the duration of tashahhud: they are waived from him. Similarly, if he forgets in making up a missed prayer, and does not prostrate until the sun becomes red. And in Friday (Jumu'ah): if its time exits. As in 'al-Fatawa'.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage presents specific cases where sajdah al-sahw is dropped due to the expiry of the prayer's time. For Fajr, if the sun rises after one has sat for tashahhud but before performing sajdah al-sahw, the sajdah is waived. For a missed prayer made up later (qada), if the sun reddens (indicating the end of 'asr time), the sajdah is dropped. Similarly for Jumu'ah if its time ends. These rulings are cited from al-Fatawa, indicating they are later authoritative positions.

Key Terms
قضاء الفائتة (qada' al-fa'itah): Making up a missed obligatory prayer after its designated time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which of the following cases is sajdah al-sahw dropped?
A) Dhuhr prayer if time ends before performing sajdah.
B) Fajr prayer if the sun rises after sitting for tashahhud.
C) Maghrib prayer if the stars appear.
D) Any prayer if the sajdah is forgotten until the next prayer.

Key Takeaway
Sajdah al-sahw is dropped if the prayer's time expires before it is performed, specifically in Fajr (after sunrise), qada (after sun reddens), and Jumu'ah (after time ends).

The Timing of Salawat and Dua After Sajdah al-Sahw: An Intra-Hanafi Analysis
Intra-Hanafi dispute on timing of salawat and dua after sajdah al-sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on when to recite salawat and dua after sajdah al-sahw, identify the preferred view, and explain its basis.

Core Idea
The place for dua is the end of prayer — salawat and dua are recited after sajdah al-sahw in the final sitting.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayra addresses a subtle question: in a prayer where sajdah al-sahw is performed, when should the worshipper send salawat upon the Prophet and make dua? While the act of sending salawat in tashahhud is a confirmed sunnah, the timing relative to the prostration of forgetfulness is subject to disagreement within the Hanafi school. The text presents the correct view and several variant opinions, allowing us to understand how later Hanafi authorities resolved this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sending salawat and making dua in the qa'dah after sajdah al-sahw. The ruling is sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah), not wajib.
This sets the context for the dispute — the hukm level is agreed upon.
2
State the mufta bihi view
The text declares 'هو الصحيح' (it is correct) to perform these after sajdah al-sahw in the final sitting. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
The evidence given: 'because the place of supplication is the end of the prayer.'
3
Present the variant opinions
Al-Tahawi held that salawat and dua should be recited in both sittings (before and after sajdah). Another transmission attributes to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that salawat is recited in the first sitting, while Muhammad al-Shaybani said it is recited in the last.
These are legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Analyse the basis of the dispute
The difference arises from interpreting the optimal placement of dua relative to the sajdah. The main view prioritizes the end of prayer, while others consider the first sitting also suitable.
No explicit rebuttal is given; the text simply asserts one view as correct.
5
Determine the practical ruling
The mufta bihi is to recite salawat and dua after sajdah al-sahw. A worshipper following any of the transmitted views is valid, but the preferred practice is after sajdah.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf – the practical consequence of the tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويأتي بالصلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، والدعاء في قعدة السهو، يعني بعد سجود السهو، هو الصحيح؛ لأن الدعاء موضعه آخر الصلاة. وقال الطحاوي: يدعو في القعدتين جميعاً، ويصلي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيهما. ومنهم من قال: عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف: يصلي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في القعدة الأولى، وعند محمد: في الأخيرة.
And he performs salawat upon the Prophet (peace be upon him) and supplication in the sitting of forgetfulness, meaning after the prostration of forgetfulness; that is the correct (view) because the place of supplication is the end of the prayer. And al-Tahawi said: He makes supplication in both sittings and sends salawat upon the Prophet in both. And among them are those who said: According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, he sends salawat upon the Prophet in the first sitting, and according to Muhammad, in the last.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage discusses where salawat and dua should be recited when sajdah al-sahw is performed. The correct view (al-sahih) is to recite them after sajdah al-sahw in the final sitting, because the place for dua is the end of prayer. Then an intra-Hanafi disagreement is presented: al-Tahawi held that they should be recited in both sittings (the one before sajdah and after). Another narration attributes to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that salawat is recited in the first sitting, while Muhammad says in the last. This shows variation among Hanafi authorities on a recommended act.

Key Terms
قعدة السهو (qa'dat al-sahw): The sitting performed after sajdah al-sahw, in which tashahhud and salam are recited.
الصلاة على النبي (al-salat 'ala al-nabi): Sending blessings upon the Prophet, e.g., 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In the first sitting before sajdah.
B) In both sittings before and after.
C) In the sitting after sajdah al-sahw.
D) They are not recommended after sajdah.

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi view is to send salawat and make dua after sajdah al-sahw because the place for dua is the end of prayer.

Effect of Saying Salam Before Sajdah al-Sahw: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on salam before sajdah al-sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions regarding salam before sajdah al-sahw and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for a follower who joins such an imam.

Core Idea
Salam before sajdah al-sahw: a suspended exit (khuruj mawquf) according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; no exit according to Muhammad and Zufar.

This passage presents a fascinating intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of saying salam when one has forgotten the sajdah al-sahw. Does the salam properly exit the prayer, or does it leave the person in a suspended state? The answer has immediate practical consequences for anyone following such an imam. Let's dissect the reasoning and see how the Hanafi masters differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage asks whether a person who says salam while owing the two sajdahs of forgetfulness (sajdah al-sahw) has exited the prayer or not.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school itself.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the person exits with a 'suspended exit' (khuruj mawquf). Muhammad and Zufar say the salam does not remove the sanctity of prayer (hurmat al-salah).
The key term is khuruj mawquf – a conditional exit.
3
Understand the concept of suspended exit
According to the first view, the salam counts as an exit, but if the person later performs sajdah al-sahw, they re-enter the state of prayer. This means the exit is provisional.
This is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply to a follower scenario
If a person says salam while owing sajdah and another person follows him as imam, according to the first view, the follower's prayer is also suspended: valid only if the imam returns to sajdah. According to the second view, the follower's prayer is valid regardless.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سلم وعليه سجدتا السهو: هل يخرج من الصلاة؟ قال أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف يخرج خروجاً موقوفاً، ثم إذا سجد للسهو : عاد إلى حرمة الصلاة. وقال محمد وزفر سلام من عليه السهو لا يُخرجه من حرمة الصلاة. وفائدته: إذا سلم وعليه سهو، فاقتدى به رجل: فاقتداؤه موقوف عندهما: إن عاد إلى سجود السهو صح اقتداؤه، وإلا فلا. وعند محمد وزفر : يصح اقتداؤه، عاد أو لم يعد.
And if he says salam while he owes the two sajdahs of forgetfulness: Does he exit the prayer? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf said: He exits with a suspended exit, then when he prostrates for forgetfulness, he returns to the sanctity of prayer. And Muhammad and Zufar said: The salam of one who owes forgetfulness does not remove him from the sanctity of prayer. And its benefit: If he says salam while he owes forgetfulness, and a man follows him, then the following is suspended according to them: if he returns to sajdah al-sahw, his following is valid; otherwise not. And according to Muhammad and Zufar, his following is valid whether he returns or not.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage addresses a significant intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the legal effect of salam when sajdah al-sahw is still owed. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf view the salam as a 'suspended exit' – the person technically exits the prayer, but if he then performs sajdah, he re-enters the prayer's sanctity. Muhammad and Zufar view the salam as ineffective – the person remains in the prayer's sanctity until sajdah is performed. The practical consequence (fā'idah) is illustrated by following such an imam: if the imam's salam is a suspended exit, the follower's prayer is also suspended; if the imam returns to sajdah, the following is valid, otherwise not. According to Muhammad and Zufar, the follower's prayer is valid regardless.

Key Terms
خروج موقوف (khuruj mawquf): A suspended exit from prayer; the salam is considered an exit but conditional on not returning to sajdah.
حرمة الصلاة (hurmat al-salah): The state of being in prayer, with all its prohibitions and obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the practical consequence (فائدة) of the dispute for a person who follows an imam who has said salam while owing sajdah al-sahw?
A) The follower's prayer is always invalid.
B) The follower's prayer is valid only if the imam returns to sajdah according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
C) The follower's prayer is always valid according to both views.
D) The follower must repeat his prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether salam before sajdah al-sahw exits the prayer; the concept of 'suspended exit' (khuruj mawquf) is key to the first view, with practical consequences for followers.

Laughter After Salam: Prayer Valid, Wudu Disputed
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wudu after laughter post-salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on laughter after salam but before sajdah al-sahw, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding wudu for the next prayer.

Core Idea
Laughter after salam does not affect prayer, but wudu depends on when hurmat al-salah ends.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah addresses a specific scenario: a person laughs (qahqahah) after saying the final salam but before performing sajdah al-sahw. You already know that laughter within prayer invalidates both the prayer and wudu. But what if the laughter occurs after the salam? The Hanafi masters agree the prayer remains valid and the sajdah al-sahw is dropped. However, they differ on whether a new wudu is required for the next prayer. This lesson unpacks that difference and its practical effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its timing
The act is qahqahah (laughter with sound) occurring after the final salam but before performing sajdah al-sahw. Recognize that the prayer has not fully ended according to some views.
Sajdah al-sahw is performed after salam for certain forgetfulness acts.
2
State the agreed-upon ruling (ijma)
All Hanafi authorities agree: the prayer is complete (sahih) and sajdah al-sahw is dropped. This consensus is based on the laughter occurring after the prayer's main acts.
The laughter does not reintroduce a sajdah obligation because it is outside the prayer proper.
3
Trace the disputed point: wudu requirement
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that no wudu is needed for the next prayer. Muhammad holds that wudu is required. The disagreement stems from an earlier ikhtilaf about the status of salam.
The earlier dispute: does salam end hurmat al-salah or just the prayer's prohibitions?
4
Understand the underlying principle
For Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, salam exits the prayer entirely, so laughter afterward does not break wudu. For Muhammad, hurmat al-salah remains until sajdah al-sahw is performed, so laughter breaks wudu.
This is a direct application of the earlier khilaf to this scenario.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text presents the view of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) as the primary position ('عندهما'). Although not explicitly stated, this is the relied-upon view.
Later Hanafi works generally follow the majority of the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قهقه بعد السلام قبل أن يسجد للسهو: فصلاته تامة، وسقط عنه السهو، إجماعاً، ولا يجب عليه الوضوء لصلاة أخرى عندهما. وقال محمد : يجب ؛ لأن القهقهة حصلت عنده في حرمة الصلاة.
And if he laughs (with sound) after the salam but before he prostrates for forgetfulness: then his prayer is complete, and the forgetfulness is dropped from him by consensus. And he is not required to perform wudu for another prayer according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). And Muhammad said: It is required, because according to him, the laughter occurred within the sanctity of prayer.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage deals with the effect of laughter (qahqahah, defined as laughter with sound) occurring after salam but before performing sajdah al-sahw. There is consensus (ijma') that the prayer is valid (complete) and the sajdah al-sahw is dropped – the laughter does not invalidate the prayer because it happened after salam. However, the need for wudu for the next prayer is disputed. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (عندهما), laughter after salam does not break wudu because the prayer has already exited, so wudu is not required. Muhammad argues that since he still considers the person to be within the hurmat al-salah (even after salam, as per his view in the previous dispute), the laughter that breaks wudu occurred within prayer, thus requiring wudu for the next prayer. This shows the practical impact of the earlier ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqahah): Laughter with sound that invalidates prayer and wudu according to Hanafi school.
إجماعاً (ijma'an): By consensus, here among Hanafi scholars on the validity of prayer and dropping of sajdah.
حرمة الصلاة (hurmat al-salah): The sanctity or inviolability of prayer; refers to the state in which certain acts are prohibited (like eating, speaking).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the consensus in this passage, what happens to the sajdah al-sahw when laughter occurs after salam but before performing it?
A) It must be performed after the laughter.
B) It is dropped and not required.
C) It becomes wajib due to the laughter.
D) The prayer must be repeated with sajdah.

Key Takeaway
Laughter after salam but before sajdah al-sahw does not affect the prayer's validity, and according to the majority Hanafi view, it does not require a new wudu for the next prayer.

Ruling on Jumu'ah When Time Exits
States a ruling for Friday prayer when its time has exited

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the time for Jumu'ah prayer has exited and identify the transmitted source for this ruling.

Core Idea
If Jumu'ah time exits, the prayer is invalid; one must pray Zuhr instead.

This terse passage from al-Jawhara al-Naira quotes a fatwa on a specific scenario: when the time for Friday prayer has passed. The text does not elaborate, but the Hanafi school's position is clear. In this lesson, we examine the ruling and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and condition
The text mentions 'Jumu'ah' and the condition 'when its time has exited'. Understand that this refers to the Friday congregational prayer and the condition that its prescribed time has elapsed.
The time for Jumu'ah in Hanafi fiqh begins at zawal and ends when the shadow of an object equals its height (same as the preferred time for Zuhr).
2
Determine the implicit ruling
Although the passage does not state the ruling explicitly, the context and Hanafi usul dictate that once Jumu'ah time exits, the prayer is no longer valid as Jumu'ah. The ruling is that one must pray Zuhr instead, as the time for Zuhr continues.
The maximum time for Zuhr in Hanafi school extends until the shadow of an object is twice its height, so Jumu'ah's exit does not mean Zuhr is missed.
3
Trace the source
The passage ends with 'كذا في «الفتاوى»' (Thus in al-Fatawa). This indicates the fatwa is transmitted in a collection of later Hanafi fatwas, likely by a scholar such as al-Karkhi or al-Ṭaḥāwī.
This shows the importance of fatwa collections in transmitting established rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الجمعة : إذا خرج وقتها. كذا في «الفتاوى».
And in Friday prayer: if its time has exited. Thus in al-Fatawa.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This is a brief fatwa stating a ruling for Friday prayer when the time has expired. The text does not elaborate on the specific ruling, but it references al-Fatawa as the source. According to Hanafi fiqh, if the time for Friday prayer ends, the prayer is no longer valid as a Jumu'ah, and one must pray Zuhr instead. The author of al-Jawhara al- Naira quotes this as a transmitted fatwa.

Key Terms
الجمعة (al-jumu'ah): The Friday congregational prayer
الفتاوى (al-fataawa): A collection of issued legal rulings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When the time for Jumu'ah exits, the prayer is invalid and must be performed as Zuhr.

Reciting Salawat and Du'a in the Sitting of Sajdah al-Sahw
Parsing an intra-Hanafi dispute on recitations in qa'dat al-sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the position of salawat and du'a in qa'dat al-sahw, identify the correct view (al-sahih), and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The correct Hanafi view: supplication belongs at the end of prayer, so recite after sajdah al-sahw.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira clarifies a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement: in which sitting during the prostration of forgetfulness (sajdah al-sahw) should one recite salawat upon the Prophet and make du'a? The text distinguishes between the first sitting (before the prostrations) and the final sitting (after), and reports varying views from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, as well as al-Tahawi. Understanding this khilaf is essential for correctly applying the fiqh of salah and for appreciating how later Hanafi authorities resolve such differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What exactly is being disputed? The timing of salawat and du'a in relation to sajdah al-sahw.
The text mentions two sittings: qa'dat al-sahw before the prostrations and after.
2
Parse the positions
List the views: al-Tahawi (both sittings), Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf (first sitting), Muhammad (last sitting), and the correct view (after prostrations).
Note the phrase 'huwa al-sahih' for the last position.
3
Analyse the dalil
The correct view's evidence: 'because supplication is placed at the end of prayer'. Why is this significant in Hanafi usul?
This is a principle about the natural order of prayer.
4
Determine the thamarah
What difference does it make in practice? If you follow the correct view, you only recite after sajdah. If you follow Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf, you recite before the prostrations; if Muhammad, after; if Tahawi, in both.
The practical consequence is the sequence of recitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويأتي بالصلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، والدعاء في قعدة السهو، يعني بعد سجود السهو، هو الصحيح؛ لأن الدعاء موضعه آخر الصلاة. وقال الطحاوي: يدعو في القعدتين جميعاً، ويصلي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيهما. ومنهم من قال: عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف: يصلي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في القعدة الأولى، وعند محمد: في الأخيرة.

And al-Tahawi said: He makes supplication in both sittings, and sends salawat upon the Prophet in both. Commentary
This passage discusses the recommended acts during the sitting after sajdah al-sahw (prostration of forgetfulness). The correct view (huwa al-sahih) is that one should recite the salawat (blessings) upon the Prophet and make du'a (supplication) after performing the two prostrations of forgetfulness, not before. The reason given is that supplication is placed at the end of prayer. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is reported: Al-Tahawi holds that one should make du'a and send salawat in both sittings (the first sitting before sajdah and the final sitting after). Others report that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say salawat in the first sitting (the one before the prostrations?), while Muhammad says in the last sitting (after the prostrations). The text does not specify which is mufta bihi, but the initial statement 'huwa al-sahih' indicates the preferred position.

Key Terms
قعدة السهو (qa'dat al-sahw): The sitting that occurs before or after the prostration of forgetfulness
الصلاة على النبي (al-salat 'ala al-nabi): Sending blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad

Effect of Salam Before Sajdah al-Sahw: Suspended Exit and Iqtida
Intra-Hanafi dispute on salam before sajdah al-sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of khuruj mawquf and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Zafar regarding the effect of salam before sajdah al-sahw on the sanctity of prayer and the validity of iqtida.

Core Idea
Salam before sajdah al-sahw creates a suspended exit: prayer sanctity is restored upon performing sajdah.

This passage explores a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: when a worshipper says the final salam before performing the obligatory prostrations for forgetfulness (sajdat al-sahw), does the prayer end? The answer determines whether a follower can validly join the prayer after that salam. We will examine the two positions and their practical consequence for iqtida.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Note the act under discussion: saying salam while sajdah al-sahw is due. The dispute is whether this salam exits the prayer or not.
Both sides are from within the Hanafi school.
2
Understand khuruj mawquf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the salam results in a suspended exit (khuruj mawquf). The person is considered to have left provisionally, but can re-enter the sanctity by performing sajdah.
This is a unique Hanafi concept.
3
Contrast with the alternative view
Muhammad and Zafar hold that the salam does not remove the sanctity of prayer (hurmat al-salah) at all; the prayer remains intact until sajdah is performed.
Thus, according to them, the salam is not effective until the wajib is fulfilled.
4
Apply to iqtida
The practical benefit (faa'idah) is shown in the case of a follower joining after salam. According to the first view, his following is suspended pending the imam's return to sajdah. If the imam performs sajdah, the following becomes valid; otherwise not. According to the second view, the following is valid immediately regardless.
This highlights the thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is whether the follower's prayer is valid from the start or conditionally valid. This affects rulings on iqtida and the imam's responsibility.
Both views are transmitted; the suspended exit view is often relied upon for caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سلم وعليه سجدتا السهو: هل يخرج من الصلاة؟ قال أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف يخرج خروجاً موقوفاً، ثم إذا سجد للسهو : عاد إلى حرمة الصلاة. وقال محمد وزفر سلام من عليه السهو لا يُخرجه من حرمة الصلاة. وفائدته: إذا سلم وعليه سهو، فاقتدى به رجل: فاقتداؤه موقوف عندهما: إن عاد إلى سجود السهو صح اقتداؤه، وإلا فلا. : وعند محمد وزفر : يصح اقتداؤه، عاد أو لم يعد.
And if he says salam while sajdat al-sahw are due to him: does he exit from the prayer? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf said: He exits with a suspended exit. Then when he prostrates for forgetfulness, he returns to the sanctity of prayer. And Muhammad and Zafar said: The salam of one who has forgetfulness due does not remove him from the sanctity of prayer. And its benefit: If he says salam while forgetfulness is upon him, and a man follows him, his following is suspended according to the two: if he returns to the prostration of forgetfulness, his following is valid; otherwise not. And according to Muhammad and Zafar: his following is valid whether he returns or not.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage addresses a key question: does saying the final salam (taslim) before performing the two prostrations of forgetfulness (sajdat al-sahw) cause one to exit the prayer completely? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the salam results in a 'suspended exit' (khuruj mawquf). The person is considered to have left the prayer provisionally, but if he subsequently performs the sajdah al-sahw, he re-enters the sanctity of prayer (hurmat al-salah). Muhammad and Zafar, however, hold that the salam does not remove the sanctity at all; the prayer remains intact until the sajdah is performed. The practical consequence (faa'idah) is illustrated with iqtida (following in prayer): if a person (e.g., a latecomer) follows this imam, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf the validity of his following is suspended pending the imam's return to sajdah. If the imam returns, the following is valid; otherwise it is void. According to Muhammad and Zafar, the following is valid regardless. This difference stems from the nature of the salam's effect when a wajib (obligatory) act like sajdah al-sahw is still due.

Key Terms
خروج موقوف (khuruj mawquf): A suspended exit from prayer: the person is considered to have left, but can re-enter the sanctity by performing the sajdah al-sahw
حرمة الصلاة (hurmat al-salah): The inviolability and legal status of being in prayer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It remains intact the whole time
B) It is lost forever
C) He returns to the sanctity of prayer after sajdah
D) He must repeat the entire prayer

Key Takeaway
The effect of salam before sajdah al-sahw is disputed: it creates a suspended exit according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and has no effect according to Muhammad and Zafar, impacting the validity of iqtida.
