باب سجود السهو
وكان عليه أن يَضُمَّ إليها ركعةً سادسة .
قوله : (وكان عليه أن يضم إليها ركعة سادسة).
- فيه إشارة إلى الوجوب.
وفي «المبسوط» (۱): قال وأَحَبُّ إليَّ أن يشفع الخامسة؛ لأن النفل شرع شفعاً، لا وتراً.
وهذا في سائر الصلوات إلا في العصر فإنه لا يضم إليها؛ لأنه يكون تطوعاً قبل المغرب، وذلك مكروه.
وفي «قاضيخان»: إلا الفجر : فإنه لا يضيف إليها؛ لأن التنفل قبلها وبعدها مكروه.
فإن اقتدى به إنسان في هاتين الركعتين، أعني الخامسة والسادسة: يلزمه ست ركعات عندهما؛ لأن الكل صار نفلاً.
وعند محمد: لا يلزمه شيء؛ لأنه قد انقطع الإحرام حين فسد الفرض، ولو لم يضم إليها ركعة سادسة: لا شيء عليه؛ لأنه مظنون، والمظنون غير مضمون، ولكن الأفضل الضم.
ثم إذا ضم: هل يسجد للسهو عندهما؟
الأصح : لا يسجد ؛ لأن النقصان بالفساد : لا يُجبر بالسجود. كذا ذكره التمرتاشي رحمه الله.
وإن قعد في الرابعة قدر التشهد ، ثم قام إلى الخامسة، ولم يسلم يظنُّها القعدة الأولى : عاد إلى القعود ما لم يسجد في الخامسة، ويسلم، ويسجد للسهو .
وإن قيد الخامسة بسجدة : ضم إليها ركعةً أخرى، وقد تمت صلاته،
قوله: (وإن قعد في الرابعة قدر التشهد ثم قام إلى الخامسة، ولم يسلم يظنُّها القعدة الأولى: عاد إلى القعود ما لم يسجد في الخامسة، ويسلم، ويسجد للسهو؛ لأن التسليم في حالة القيام غير مشروع في الصلاة المطلقة.
فإن سلم قائماً : لا تفسد صلاته، ولو عاد: لا يعيد التشهد. قوله : ( فإن قيد الخامسة بسجدة: ضم إليها ركعةً أخرى، وقد تمت صلاته).
فإن قلت: هل ضمُّ الأخرى على الإيجاب، أم على الاستحباب؟ قلتُ: ذَكَرَ في «الأصل» ما يدل على الوجوب، فإنه قال: وعليه أن يضم وكلمة على : للإيجاب.
- ثم إذا أضاف إليها أخرى فإنه يتشهد ويسلم، ويسجد للسهو؛ لأنه ترك لفظة السلام، وكان القياس أن لا يجب عليه سجود السهو؛ لأن سهوه وقع في الفرض، وقد انتقل منه إلى النفل
Chapter of Prostration of Forgetfulness. And it is upon him to add a sixth rak'ah to it. His statement: (And it is upon him to add a sixth rak'ah). - In it is an indication of obligation. In al-Mabsut: He said: And it is more beloved to me that he makes the fifth even (by adding a sixth) because voluntary prayer is prescribed in pairs, not odd. And this applies to all prayers except Asr, for he does not add to it, because it would be a voluntary prayer before Maghrib, and that is disliked. And in Qadikhan: Except Fajr, for he does not add to it, because voluntary prayer before it and after it is disliked. If a person follows him in these two rak'ahs, namely the fifth and sixth: six rak'ahs become obligatory upon him according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because the entire (prayer) became voluntary. And according to Muhammad: nothing becomes obligatory upon him, because the ihram was severed when the obligatory prayer became invalid. And if he does not add a sixth rak'ah: nothing is upon him, because it is conjectural, and the conjectural is not guaranteed, but the better is to add. Then if he adds: Does he prostrate for forgetfulness according to them? The correct (view) is that he does not prostrate, because the deficiency due to invalidity is not compensated by prostration. As mentioned by al-Tamartashi, may Allah have mercy on him. And if he sat in the fourth (rak'ah) for the duration of tashahhud, then stood for the fifth, and did not say salam, thinking it is the first sitting: he returns to sitting as long as he has not prostrated in the fifth, and says salam, and prostrates for forgetfulness. And if he performed a sajdah (in the fifth): he adds another rak'ah to it, and his prayer becomes complete. His statement: (And if he sat in the fourth for the duration of tashahhud then stood for the fifth, and did not say salam, thinking it is the first sitting: he returns to sitting as long as he has not prostrated in the fifth, and says salam, and prostrates for forgetfulness) because saying salam while standing is not prescribed in absolute prayer. If he says salam while standing: his prayer does not become invalid, and if he returns, he does not repeat tashahhud. His statement: (If he performed a sajdah in the fifth: he adds another rak'ah to it, and his prayer becomes complete). If you say: Is the addition of the other (rak'ah) obligatory or recommended? I say: He mentioned in al-Asl what indicates obligation, for he said: 'And it is upon him to add' and the word 'upon' indicates obligation. - Then if he adds another (rak'ah) to it, he recites tashahhud and says salam, and prostrates for forgetfulness, because he left the words of salam. And the analogy was that prostration for forgetfulness should not be obligatory upon him, because his forgetfulness occurred in the obligatory (prayer), and he has transferred from it to voluntary (prayer).
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah, a commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, discusses rulings related to prostration of forgetfulness. The author first establishes that when a person mistakenly stands for a fifth rak'ah in a four-rak'ah prayer, it is obligatory (wajib) to add a sixth rak'ah to make the voluntary prayer even (shaf'). This is based on the principle that voluntary prayer is prescribed in pairs. The text notes exceptions: for Asr prayer, adding a fifth and sixth is disliked because it becomes voluntary before Maghrib; for Fajr, because voluntary before and after Fajr is disliked. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: if someone follows the imam in these two rak'ahs, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) he must perform six rak'ahs (the original four plus the two) since the entire prayer becomes voluntary. According to Muhammad alone, nothing is obligatory because the ihram was broken when the fard became invalid. If one does not add a sixth rak'ah, there is no penalty because the matter is conjectural, but adding is better. On the question of whether sajdat al-sahw is performed after adding, the correct view (al-sahih) according to al-Tamartashi is no, because the deficiency was not due to a mistake within a valid prayer but due to invalidity. The passage then considers another scenario: if a person sits for tashahhud in the fourth rak'ah and then stands for a fifth without saying salam, thinking it is the first sitting, he must return to sitting as long as he has not prostrated in the fifth, then say salam and perform sajdat al-sahw. If he has already prostrated in the fifth, he adds another rak'ah (making a sixth) and his prayer is complete. The commentator clarifies that the addition of the sixth rak'ah is obligatory based on the wording in al-Asl. Finally, if he adds another rak'ah, he recites tashahhud, says salam, and performs sajdat al-sahw because he omitted the words of salam. The original analogy would have required no sajdat al-sahw since the mistake occurred in the fard and he moved to nafl, but istihsan overrides that.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sujūd al-sahw): Prostration performed to compensate for forgetfulness in prayer.
ضم (ḍamm): Adding a rak'ah to make the number even (shaf).
شفع (shaf‘): Making a number even; pairing rak'ahs.
مظنون (maẓnūn): Conjectural; based on probability rather than certainty.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides analogy (qiyas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what must a person do if they mistakenly stand for a fifth rak'ah in a four-rak'ah fard prayer?
A) Return to sitting immediately and perform sajdat al-sahw.
B) Add a sixth rak'ah to make the voluntary prayer even.
C) Leave the prayer and restart.
D) Continue with the fifth rak'ah and add a sixth only if they wish.

Key Takeaway
When you mistakenly stand for a fifth in a four-rak'ah prayer, add a sixth rak'ah to make the voluntary prayer even — it is wajib.

Ruling on Mistakenly Standing for a Fifth Rak'ah: Correction and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Ruling on mistaken fifth rak'ah with correction and ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when someone mistakenly stands for a fifth rak'ah, apply the correction method, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the follower's obligation.

Core Idea
An extra rak'ah can be corrected by returning unless a prostration occurs; then it becomes a two-rak'at nafil with wajib sajdat al-sahw.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira on the mistake of standing for a fifth rak'ah in a four-rak'ah prayer. The passage details the required corrective actions and the underlying reasoning of istihsan. It also presents a notable disagreement between Imam Muhammad and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the follower who joins the imam during the extra rak'ahs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistake and the two scenarios
Recognize that the worshiper mistakenly stands for a fifth rak'ah after sitting for tashahhud in the fourth rak'ah. The ruling depends on whether he has already performed a prostration in the fifth rak'ah or not.
The scenario assumes the worshiper did not say the greeting (salam) before standing.
2
Apply the first scenario (no prostration yet)
If he has not yet prostrated in the fifth rak'ah, he must return to the sitting position, say the greeting, and then perform the prostration of forgetfulness (sajdat al-sahw).
The return is valid as long as he has not prostrated; after prostration, the rak'ah is considered complete.
3
Apply the second scenario (prostration occurred)
If he has performed a prostration in the fifth rak'ah, he cannot return; instead, he must add another rak'ah (making the fifth and sixth a two-rak'ah unit), then recite tashahhud, greet, and perform sajdat al-sahw.
This addition is wajib (obligatory) as indicated by the word 'عليه' in Al-Asl.
4
Understand the legal reasoning (istihsan)
The ruling that sajdat al-sahw is required despite the transition from obligatory to supererogatory prayer is based on istihsan. The original tahrima (intention) unifies the entire prayer, so the forgetfulness is considered as occurring within one continuous worship.
Strict analogy (qiyas) would not require sajdat al-sahw, but istihsan overrides it.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad holds that a follower (muqtadi) who joins during the extra two rak'ahs must make up six rak'ahs if his prayer is invalidated, because the imam's original tahrima is not severed. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the follower only owes two rak'ahs, because the imam has exited the obligatory prayer. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two, requiring only two rak'ahs.
The practical benefit (thamarah) is the difference in qada: 6 vs. 2 rak'ahs.

قال في الوجيز»: وهو الأصح؛ لأن إحرام الفرض لما لم ينقطع عنده: صار المقتدي شارعاً في الكل، فلزمه ما أدى الإمام بهذه التحريمة، وقد أدى ستاً.

Chapter of the Prostration of Forgetfulness. And if he sits in the fourth to the measure of the tashahhud, then stands for the fifth, and does not greet, thinking it is the first sitting: he returns to the sitting as long as he has not prostrated in the fifth, and he greets, and he prostrates for forgetfulness. And if he prostrates in the fifth: he adds another rak'ah to it, and his prayer is complete. * His statement: (And if he sits in the fourth to the measure of tashahhud, then stands for the fifth, and does not greet, thinking it is the first sitting: he returns to the sitting as long as he has not prostrated in the fifth, and he greets, and he prostrates for forgetfulness; because greeting while standing is not legislated in the absolute prayer. If he greets while standing: his prayer is not invalidated, and if he returns: he does not repeat the tashahhud. His statement: (And if he performs a prostration in the fifth: he adds another rak'ah to it, and his prayer is complete). For if you say: Is this addition obligatory or recommended? I say: He mentioned in Al-Asl what indicates obligation, for he said: It is incumbent upon him to add, and the word 'upon' indicates obligation. Then when he adds another to it, he recites the tashahhud and greets, and prostrates for forgetfulness; because he omitted the greeting word, and the analogy was that the prostration of forgetfulness should not be obligatory upon him, because his forgetfulness occurred in the obligatory prayer, and he had transitioned from it to supererogatory prayer. And whoever forgets in a prayer: it is not obligatory upon him to prostrate in another prayer, except that the first is istihsan, and its reasoning is that his transition to supererogatory prayer is based on the first tahrima, so in regard to forgetfulness, it is as if they were one prayer. If someone follows him in these two rak'ahs: he is obliged to (complete) six? He makes up, according to Muhammad. He said in Al-Wajiz: And it is the more correct; because the beginning of the obligatory prayer has not been severed according to him, so the follower becomes a participant in the whole, so he is obliged to what the imam performed with this tahrima, and he has performed six. According to the two, he is obliged to two rak'ahs; because he followed him in supererogatory prayer after his exit from the obligatory. If the follower invalidates: there is no making up upon him, according to Muhammad; and according to the two, he makes up two rak'ahs, and that is the correct opinion, and upon it is the fatwa. * His statement: (And he prostrates for forgetfulness) in consideration of the imam. This prostration is for the defect present in the supererogatory according to Abu Yusuf; because he entered into it not in the legislated manner. And according to Muhammad: for the defect present in the obligatory, which is his exit from it not in the legislated manner. And its benefit is for the one who follows him: according to Abu Yusuf, upon the follower is making up two rak'ahs; because his exit from the obligatory has become firm, and the defect is only in the supererogatory. And according to Muhammad: he makes up six; because what is performed with this tahrima is six. * His statement: (And the two rak'ahs are supererogatory for him.)
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the ruling for someone who mistakenly stands for a fifth rak'ah after sitting for the tashahhud in a four-rak'ah prayer, thinking it was the first sitting. The ruling: if he has not yet prostrated in the fifth, he must return to the sitting, say the greeting, and then perform the prostration of forgetfulness (sajdat al-sahw). If he has already prostrated in the fifth, he must add another rak'ah to it (making the fifth and sixth a two-rak'ah unit), and his prayer is considered complete. The commentator notes that this addition is obligatory (wajib) based on the original text (Al-Asl). The reasoning (istihsan) is that the original intention (tahrima) connects the extra rak'ahs to the obligatory prayer, making them part of one worship. A significant intra-Hanafi dispute arises regarding the follower (muqtadi) who joins the imam during these two extra rak'ahs. According to Imam Muhammad, the follower must complete six rak'ahs in total, because the imam's original obligatory prayer is still valid, and the follower is participating in the entire prayer from the initial takbir. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two), the follower only owes two rak'ahs (the extra two), because the imam has already exited the obligatory prayer. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two, requiring the follower to make up two rak'ahs if he invalidates his prayer. The passage also discusses the nature of the prostration for forgetfulness: Abu Yusuf considers it for a defect in the supererogatory part, while Muhammad considers it for a defect in the obligatory part.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sajdat al-sahw): The prostration of forgetfulness performed to compensate for errors in prayer, such as omitting a wajib or adding a rak'ah.
التحريمة (al-tahrima): The initial takbir (takbirat al-ihram) that begins the prayer; by extension, the entire intention and framework of the prayer.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juridical preference overriding strict analogy; here, treating the extra rak'ah as connected to the original prayer based on the unity of the tahrima.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person mistakenly stands for a fifth rak'ah after the tashahhud in the fourth rak'ah and prostrates in the fifth, what must he do?
A) Return to the sitting and perform sajdat al-sahw.
B) Add another rak'ah (making two rak'ah) and complete the prayer with sajdat al-sahw.
C) Abandon the prayer and start over.
D) Continue the fifth rak'ah as normal and add sajdat al-sahw.

Key Takeaway
A mistaken fifth rak'ah can be corrected by returning if no prostration, or by adding a sixth rak'ah if prostration occurred, both cases requiring sajdat al-sahw, with the follower's obligation being only two rak'ahs according to the relied-upon position.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Follower's Qada in Sujud al-Sahw
Explains ruling on follower joining imam in extra rak'ahs due to forgetfulness, with direct ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the follower's qada in sujud al-sahw and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
When a follower joins an imam in extra nafl rak'ahs, the qada depends on whether the tahrima links him to the entire prayer or only the nafl.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses a practical scenario: an imam prays two extra rak'ahs due to forgetfulness, and a follower joins him. Scholars differ on how many rak'ahs the follower must make up. This dispute illustrates the interplay between the follower's intention and the imam's action, and shows how the Hanafi school resolves internal disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The imam prays two extra rak'ahs (nafl) due to forgetfulness. A follower joins him in these rak'ahs. Determine what the follower must do.
The follower's prayer is linked to the imam's tahrima.
2
State the position of Imam Muhammad
According to Muhammad, the follower must make up six rak'ahs because his tahrima made him a participant in the entire prayer performed by the imam (4 fard + 2 nafl = 6).
Muhammad: qada is wajib, and the follower performed 6 rak'ahs through the imam.
3
State the position of the two (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf)
The two hold that the follower only makes up two rak'ahs because he joined after the imam exited the fard; thus he only participated in the nafl portion.
The two: the follower's prayer is only the 2 rak'ahs he actually performed.
4
Analyze the basis of the difference
The dispute rests on how the follower's intention (tahrima) relates to the imam's actions. Muhammad sees continuity; the two see a separation after fard is complete.
The difference in conceptualizing the follower's link to the imam's prayer.
5
Determine the mufta bihi view
The text explicitly states that the view of the two is 'asahh' (most correct) and that fatwa is given accordingly: the follower makes up 2 rak'ahs, even if he invalidates his own prayer.
The relied-upon view is the two's view.

Source Text

* قوله : (ويسجد للسهو) .

* قوله : (والركعتان له نافلة) .
Chapter of Sujud al-Sahw. The two rak'ahs are supererogatory for him, and he prostrates for forgetfulness. The first, in the case of forgetfulness, it is as if they are one prayer. Muhammad: If someone follows him in these two rak'ahs, it becomes incumbent upon him to pray six [rak'ahs] as make-up, according to Muhammad. He said in al-Wajiz: This is the most correct; because the ihram of the obligatory prayer was not severed according to him; thus the follower became a participant in all, so what the imam performed with this tahrima becomes incumbent upon him, and he performed six. According to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], two rak'ahs become incumbent upon him because he followed him in supererogatory after exiting the obligatory. If the follower invalidates [his prayer], there is no make-up upon him according to Muhammad; according to the two, he makes up two rak'ahs, and this is the correct [view], and on it is the fatwa. * His saying: 'And he prostrates for forgetfulness.' In consideration of the imam, and this prostration is for the deficiency that occurred in the supererogatory according to Abu Yusuf, because he entered it not in the prescribed manner. According to Muhammad, it is for the deficiency that occurred in the obligatory, because he exited it not in the prescribed manner. And its benefit concerning the one who followed him: According to Abu Yusuf, the follower must make up two rak'ahs because his exit from the obligatory was confirmed, and the deficiency is only in the supererogatory. According to Muhammad, he makes up six because it is what was performed with this tahrima. * His saying: 'And the two rak'ahs are supererogatory for him.'
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira is a commentary on the chapter of Sujud al-Sahw. It first states that when an imam prays two rak'ahs due to forgetfulness (thinking his prayer is incomplete), those two are considered nafl (supererogatory) for him. However, in terms of forgetfulness, they are treated as if continuous with the fard. Then it discusses a dispute: if someone follows the imam in these two rak'ahs, what must he make up? According to Imam Muhammad (in Zahir al-Riwayah), the follower must make up six rak'ahs because he intended to follow the imam from the start, and by following, he entered into the entire prayer (which became 6 rak'ahs). The two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say two rak'ahs because the follower joined after the imam had exited the fard, so he only followed him in nafl. Then if the follower invalidates his own prayer, Muhammad says no qada (because his prayer was supererogatory and invalid, no make-up), but the two say he must make up two. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the two's view: qada two. Then the commentary addresses the sujud al-sahw: why does the imam prostrate? Abu Yusuf says it's for the deficiency in nafl, Muhammad says for the deficiency in fard (since he exited fard incorrectly). This leads to practical difference: if someone followed, according to Abu Yusuf he makes up two, according to Muhammad six. The passage concludes by reiterating that the two rak'ahs are nafl for the imam.

Key Terms
تحريمة (tahrima): The initial takbir (Allahu Akbar) that initiates the prayer; the consecration.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed or invalidated prayer after the fact.
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory prayer; voluntary act of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, if a follower joins an imam in the two extra rak'ahs that the imam prays due to forgetfulness, how many rak'ahs must the follower make up?
A) Six
B) Two
C) Four
D) None

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi view is that a follower who joins an imam in extra nafl rak'ahs due to forgetfulness must make up only 2 rak'ahs.

Doubt in Prayer: First Occurrence vs. Habitual Doubt
States the ruling for doubt in prayer, distinguishing first-time from frequent doubt, and defines related terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between first-time and habitual doubt in prayer, apply the appropriate Hanafi ruling (restart or build on predominant belief/certainty), and define the key terms shakk, zann, and wahm.

Core Idea
Doubt in prayer: first occurrence requires restart; for habitual doubters, rely on predominant belief or certainty.

This lesson from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the Hanafi approach to doubt during prayer. When a worshipper cannot recall the number of rak'ahs, the ruling depends on whether this is a one-time event or a recurring problem. The passage also defines three crucial epistemic states—shakk (true doubt), zann (predominant belief), and wahm (illusion)—and includes a valuable intra-Hanafi discussion on what constitutes 'first time.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of doubt
Determine whether the doubt is occurring for the first time (or rarely) or it is a frequent, habitual occurrence. The text distinguishes between 'first time it happened' and 'doubt happens to him frequently.'
The classification determines the entire subsequent ruling.
2
Apply the ruling
If first-time, restart the whole prayer (istinaf). If frequent, act on predominant belief (zann); if no predominant belief, build on certainty (yaqin) by assuming the lower number of rak'ahs.
Building on certainty means taking the fewer rak'ahs (e.g., assume 3 if unsure between 3 and 4).
3
Define the three epistemic states
Learn the technical meanings: shakk (equal doubt, no advantage), zann (correctness more likely), wahm (error more likely). These definitions govern how the worshipper should proceed.
The commentary emphasizes that wahm is given no weight; only zann or yaqin are considered.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi nuance on 'first time'
Understand that 'first time' is interpreted differently: some say in the person's entire life, others say in that particular prayer. Shams al-A'imma's preferred view is that it means forgetfulness is not habitual. This affects cases where years pass between rare doubts.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position follows Shams al-A'imma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن شك في صلاته، فلم يدر أثلاثاً صلى أم أربعاً؟ وكان ذلك أول ما عرض له : استأنف الصلاة .
فإن كان الشك يعرض له كثيراً : بنى على غالب ظنه إن كان له ظن.
فإن لم يكن له ظن : بنى على اليقين .
ولا ينوبان عن سنة الظهر، على الصحيح؛ لأنهما مظنونتان، والمظنون:
أحكام الشك في الصلاة :]
قوله: (ومن شك في صلاته، فلم يدر أثلاثاً صلى، أم أربعاً؟ وكان ذلك أول ما عرض له : استأنف الصلاة.
وإن كان الشك يعرض له كثيراً : بنى على غالب ظنه إن كان له ظن،
فإن لم يكن له ظن: بنى على اليقين).
الشك : تساوي الأمرين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر. والظن: تساوي الأمرين وجهة الصواب أرجح.
والوهم: تساوي الأمرين، وجهة الخطأ أرجح.
قوله : أول ما عرض له قيل في عمره، وقيل: في الصلاة.
وقال شمس الأئمة : معناه: لم يكن السهو من عادته.
وفائدته : إذا سها في صلاته أول مرة واستقبل، ثم وقف سنين، ثم سها: على قول شمس الأئمة: يستأنف ؛ لأنه لم يكن من عادته، وإنما
And whoever doubts in his prayer, not knowing whether he prayed three or four [rak'ahs]? And that was the first time it happened to him: he restarts the prayer. If doubt happens to him frequently: he builds on his predominant belief if he has a belief. If he has no belief: he builds on certainty. And they do not suffice for the sunnah of Dhuhr, according to the correct [view]; because they are conjectured, and the conjectured: Rulings of Doubt in Prayer. His saying: (And whoever doubts in his prayer, not knowing whether he prayed three or four? And that was the first time it happened to him: he restarts the prayer. And if doubt happens to him frequently: he builds on his predominant belief if he has a belief, if he has no belief: he builds on certainty). Doubt: equality of two matters with no advantage for one over the other. And belief: equality of two matters with the side of correctness being more likely. And illusion: equality of two matters with the side of error being more likely. His saying: 'first time it happened to him' — it is said: in his lifetime, and it is said: in the prayer. And Shams al-A'imma said: its meaning is that forgetfulness was not his habit. And its benefit: if he forgets in his prayer for the first time and restarts, then years pass, then he forgets: according to the statement of Shams al-A'imma, he restarts because it is not his habit, and indeed...
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Jawhara al-Nayyira presents the Hanafi ruling on doubt (shakk) during prayer. It begins by citing the matn: if a person doubts the number of rak'ahs (e.g., whether he prayed three or four) and this is the first time such doubt occurs in his lifetime, he must restart the entire prayer (istinaf). However, if doubt is frequent (i.e., he suffers from waswasah or habitual forgetfulness), he should act on his predominant belief (ghalib al-zann). If he has no predominant belief (i.e., the two possibilities are completely equal), he must build on certainty (yaqin) — that is, assume the lower number. The matn also notes that these two rak'ahs (the extra ones prayed due to doubt) do not substitute for the sunnah of Dhuhr, because they are conjectured. The commentary then defines the key terms: shakk is true equality of two possibilities; zann is where correctness is more likely; wahm is where error is more likely. On the phrase 'first time it happened to him', the commentator notes differing interpretations: some say it means in his entire life, others say in that particular prayer. Shams al-A'imma clarifies that it means forgetfulness was not his habit. From this, the ruling emerges: if he forgets for the first time and restarts, then after years forgets again, according to Shams al-A'imma, he still restarts because it is still not his habit. This demonstrates the nuanced Hanafi approach to dealing with doubt in worship.

Key Terms
شك (shakk): Equal doubt between two possibilities with no advantage for one over the other (e.g., three vs four rak'ahs).
ظن (zann): Predominant belief where the side of correctness is more likely than error.
وهم (wahm): Illusion where the side of error is more likely; no weight is given to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what should a person do if he experiences doubt in the number of rak'ahs of prayer for the first time in his life?
A) He builds on his predominant belief (zann) if he has one, otherwise assumes the lower number.
B) He continues based on certainty (yaqin) by assuming the lower number.
C) He repeats the entire prayer (restarts).
D) He performs prostrations of forgetfulness (sujud al-sahw) and continues.

Key Takeaway
First-time doubt in prayer requires restarting; frequent doubters must act on predominant belief (zann) or certainty (yaqin).

Ruling on Doubt (Shakk) in Prayer: First-Time vs. Habitual
Ruling on doubt in prayer with definitions of shakk, zann, wahm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling for doubt in prayer by distinguishing first-time from habitual doubt and determining when to build on predominant assumption or certainty.

Core Idea
First-time doubt invalidates the prayer; habitual doubt is resolved by building on predominant assumption, otherwise on certainty.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on doubt (shakk) during prayer. You will learn the key distinction between a first-time occurrence and a habitually doubting person. The passage defines three mental states—shakk, zann, and wahm—and provides a clear procedure for the worshipper when uncertainty arises. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly handling common situations in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Classify the doubt as first-time or habitual
Determine whether this doubt has occurred to the person before. The text distinguishes between 'first time' and 'frequently occurring' doubt. This classification determines the subsequent ruling.
The commentary discusses varying interpretations of 'first time'—in one's life or in that prayer.
2
Check for a predominant assumption (ghalib al-zann)
If the doubt is habitual, the next step is to see if the person has a predominant assumption about the number of rak'ahs. If he leans more toward a particular number (e.g., feels it is more likely he prayed three), he acts on that.
Zann is defined as equality of two possibilities but with the side of correctness more likely.
3
Apply the ruling
For first-time doubt: restart the entire prayer (istinaf). For habitual doubt with predominant assumption: build on that assumption and complete the prayer. For habitual doubt without any assumption: build on certainty (the smaller number, e.g., three).
This follows the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt.
4
Learn the technical definitions
Memorize the three terms: shakk (equal doubt), zann (preponderance of correctness), and wahm (preponderance of error). These are used to describe the strength of one's belief.
Wahm is not acted upon; only zann and yaqin are actionable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب سجود السهو
ومَن شك في صلاته، فلم يَدْرِ أثلاثاً صلَّى أم أربعاً؟ وكان ذلك أول ما عَرَضَ له : استأنف الصلاة.
فإن كان الشكُ يَعْرِضُ له كثيراً : بنى على غالب ظنّه إن كان له ظن.
فإن لم يكن له ظن : بنى على اليقين.
الشك : تساوي الأمرين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر. والظن: تساوي الأمرين وجهة الصواب أرجح.
والوهم: تساوي الأمرين، وجهة الخطأ أرجح.
قوله : أول ما عرض له قيل في عمره، وقيل: في الصلاة.
وقال شمس الأئمة : معناه: لم يكن السهو من عادته.
وفائدته : إذا سها في صلاته أول مرة واستقبل، ثم وقف سنين، ثم سها: على قول شمس الأئمة: يستأنف ؛ لأنه لم يكن من عادته، وإنما حصل عليه مرة واحدة، والعادة إنما هي من المعاودة. وعلى العبارتين الأوليين: يجتهد في ذلك.
وقوله : بنى على اليقين وهو الأقل، والله تعالى أعلم.
Chapter of the Prostration of Forgetfulness. Whoever doubts in his prayer and does not know whether he prayed three or four? And that was the first time it occurred to him: he restarts the prayer. If doubt occurs to him frequently: he builds on his predominant assumption if he has an assumption. If he has no assumption: he builds on certainty. Doubt: equality of two matters with no preference of one over the other. Assumption: equality of two matters but the side of correctness is more likely. Illusion: equality of two matters, but the side of error is more likely. His statement: 'first time it occurred to him' — it is said: in his life, and it is said: in the prayer. Shams al-A'imma said: its meaning is that forgetfulness was not his habit. Its benefit: if he forgets in his prayer the first time and starts over, then stops for years, then forgets: according to the statement of Shams al-A'imma, he restarts because it was not his habit, and it only happened once, and habit is only from repetition. According to the first two expressions: he exerts effort in that. His statement: 'he builds on certainty' and it is the lesser [number]. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Sujud al-Sahw deals with the ruling of doubt (shakk) in prayer. The author first states the matn from al-Quduri: if a person doubts whether he prayed three or four rak'ahs and it is the first time this has happened to him, he must restart the prayer (istinaf). If doubt occurs to him frequently, he builds on his predominant assumption (ghalib al-zann) if he has one; otherwise, he builds on certainty (yaqin), which is the smaller number (three). The commentator then defines three mental states: shakk (equal doubt), zann (preponderance of correctness), and wahm (preponderance of error). He also discusses the interpretation of 'first time': is it in his life or in that specific prayer? Shams al-A'imma holds it means it is not his habit. The benefit of this is that if he forgets once, stops for years, then forgets again, according to Shams al-A'imma he still restarts because it is not habitual; others say he must exert effort to determine. The ruling is based on the principle of building on certainty (al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk).

Key Terms
شك (shakk): Equality of two possibilities with no preference for either side.
ظن (zann): Equality of two possibilities but the side of correctness is more likely.
وهم (wahm): Equality of two possibilities but the side of error is more likely.
غالب الظن (ghalib al-zann): Predominant assumption that overcomes doubt.
اليقين (yaqin): Certainty; the lesser number (e.g., three rak'ahs) when no assumption exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
First-time doubt requires restarting the prayer; habitual doubt is resolved by building on predominant assumption, otherwise on certainty.

Ruling on the Number of Prostrations of Forgetfulness
Concluding remarks on sajdah al-sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that one sajdah al-sahw is sufficient for forgetfulness in a single prayer, and apply the principle of relying on certainty (al-yaqīn) to determine the minimum.

Core Idea
When in doubt, one prostration of forgetfulness suffices; certainty dictates the lesser number.

This concluding passage from a Sharh of al-Quduri addresses a subtle point: if a person forgets multiple times during one prayer, how many prostrations of forgetfulness (sajdah al-sahw) are required? The text resolves the matter by invoking the Hanafi legal maxim that certainty is not overridden by doubt. The commentator clarifies that only one sajdah is owed, and that the worshipper should not multiply prostrations based on repeated forgetfulness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
The passage addresses whether multiple instances of forgetfulness in one prayer require multiple prostrations or just one. Note that the earlier part of the commentary (not shown) had two possible expressions that left the matter to ijtihad.
The key phrase is 'حصل عليه مرة واحدة' – it occurs upon him once.
2
Apply the Principle of Habit (al-ʿādah)
The text states that 'habit is only from repetition' – meaning repeated forgetfulness does not create a new obligation; the ruling remains one prostration per prayer.
This uses ʿādah as a secondary source in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the Maxim of Certainty (al-yaqīn)
The commentator concludes by citing the principle: 'built on certainty, which is the least.' When there is doubt about the number of prostrations, one takes the minimum (one) because certainty is not removed by doubt.
This is the Hanafi maxim: al-yaqīn lā yazūlu bi al-shakk.
4
Derive the Ruling
Therefore, regardless of how many times forgetfulness occurs during a single prayer, only one sajdah al-sahw (consisting of two prostrations) is required. The worshipper should not perform multiple sets.
This ruling is muftā bihī in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حصل عليه مرة واحدة، والعادة إنما هي من المعاودة. وعلى العبارتين الأوليين: يجتهد في ذلك. وقوله : بنى على اليقين وهو الأقل، والله تعالى أعلم.
It occurs upon him once, and habit is only from repetition. And upon the two first expressions: he strives in that. And his saying: built on certainty which is the least, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the commentator. It addresses a dispute regarding the number of prostrations of forgetfulness (sajdah al-sahw). The text clarifies that the sajdah is performed once, not multiple times, even if forgetfulness occurs repeatedly. The principle 'habit is from repetition' indicates that repeated occurrences do not change the ruling; one sajdah suffices for each instance. The phrase 'upon the two first expressions' refers to earlier formulations in the commentary where the matter was left to ijtihad. The commentator concludes by invoking the legal maxim: 'built on certainty, which is the least,' meaning in cases of doubt one defaults to the smaller number (i.e., one prostration).

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sujūd al-sahw): Prostration performed to compensate for forgetfulness or mistake in prayer.
العادة (al-ʿādah): Habit; here used in legal context meaning customary recurrence.
اليقين (al-yaqīn): Certainty; legal principle that certainty is not removed by doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Abundance
B) Custom
C) Certainty which is the least
D) Ijtihad

Key Takeaway
One sajdah al-sahw suffices for any number of forgetfulness in a single prayer because certainty dictates the minimum.

Ruling on the Prayer of the Sick When Unable to Stand
Opening of the chapter on the sick person's prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for the sick person who cannot stand, define the correct criterion for excusing standing (harm), and explain the rule of partial ability (stand as much as you can).

Core Idea
If standing causes harm, the sick pray sitting—but they must stand as much as they can.

The norm in obligatory prayer is to stand. But when illness makes standing harmful or impossible, the law grants a concession: pray sitting. However, this concession is not absolute—any portion of standing you can manage must be performed. This lesson unpacks the precise criterion (harm, not mere dizziness) and the rule of partial ability, as taught by Imam al-Quduri and his commentator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Basic Ruling
Read the first sentence: 'If standing is unattainable for the sick person, he prays sitting, bowing and prostrating.' Note that ruku and sujud remain normal; only qiyam is replaced by sitting.
The ruling applies only when standing is genuinely unattainable (ta'adhdhar).
2
Understand the Criterion (the Dispute)
The commentary mentions differing views: some said falling due to weakness/dizziness; the most correct (al-asahh) is that standing would cause harm (darar). Understand why 'harm' is broader and preferred.
Harm includes pain, exacerbation of illness, or risk of injury—not just falling.
3
Apply the Rule of Partial Ability
If the sick person can stand partially but not fully, he must stand for the portion he is able. The text gives the example: if he can say the opening takbir standing but cannot stand for recitation, he says takbir standing, then sits.
This shows the obligation is not completely dropped—only proportionally.
4
Note the Placement of the Chapter
The author placed this chapter after forgetfulness because both are accidental matters (awarid). Forgetfulness is more frequent and thus more important. This contextualization aids memory.
The expansion probe style shows why the ordering matters in the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة المريض إذا تعذر على المريض القيام : صلَّى قاعداً، يركع ويسجد. باب صلاة المريض إنما ذَكَرَه عقيب السهو؛ لأن كلا منهما من العوارض، إلا أن السهو أكثر: فكان أهم؛ لأنه يتناول صلاة الصحيح والمريض، فقدمه عليه؛ لشدة مساس الحاجة إلى بيانه. ثم إضافته : إضافة الفعل إلى فاعله، كـ: قيام زيد. ويسجد). قال رحمه الله : (إذا تعذر على المريض القيام: صلى قاعداً، اختلفوا في حد المرض الذي يبيح له الصلاة قاعداً: فقيل: أن يكون بحال إذا قام سقط من ضعف، أو دوران الرأس، والأصح أن يكون بحيث يلحقه بالقيام ضرر. وإذا كان قادراً على بعض القيام، دون تمامه: أمر بأن يقوم مقدار ما يقدر. فإذا عجز : قعد، حتى لو قدر أن يكبر قائماً للتحريمة، ولم يقدر على القيام، يعني للقراءة، أو كان يقدر على القيام لبعض القراءة، دون تمامها: فإنه يُؤمر أن يكبر قائماً، ويقرأ ما يقدر عليه قائماً، ثم يقعد إذا عجز.
Chapter on the prayer of the sick. If standing is unattainable for the sick person, he prays sitting, bowing and prostrating. The author mentioned it after the chapter on forgetfulness because each of them is from accidental matters, but forgetfulness is more frequent, so it was more important because it concerns the prayer of the healthy and the sick. Then its addition is the addition of the action to its doer, like 'the standing of Zayd'. And he prostrates. He said: 'If standing is unattainable for the sick person, he prays sitting.' They differed about the definition of illness that permits him to pray sitting: it was said that he is in a state where if he stands he falls due to weakness or dizziness, and the most correct is that it is such that standing would cause him harm. If he is able to stand partially, he is ordered to stand as much as he can. When unable, he sits. For example, if he can say the opening takbir standing but is unable to stand for recitation, or he can stand for some recitation, he says the takbir standing, recites what he can standing, then sits when unable.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the prayer of the sick from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The commentator explains why the prostration chapter precedes: both deal with accidental matters, but forgetfulness is more common and affects everyone. The core ruling: if standing is impossible due to illness, the sick person prays sitting while still performing ruku' and sujud normally. There is a disagreement on the exact definition of a valid excuse: some say it's when standing leads to falling or dizziness, but the most correct view (al-asahh) is when standing causes harm (darar). If the person can stand partially, he must stand for the portion he can. For instance, he should say the opening takbir standing, then sit if unable to stand for recitation. This reflects the Hanafi principle that the obligation to stand is only dropped proportionally to inability.

Key Terms
تعذر (taʿadhdhar): To be unattainable; here meaning standing is not feasible due to illness.
العوارض (al-ʿawāriḍ): Accidental matters, here forgetfulness and illness.
التحريمة (al-taḥrīmah): The opening takbir which initiates the prayer.
ضرر (ḍarar): Harm; the criterion for excusing standing (al-asahh).
قاعداً (qāʿidan): Sitting; the posture for prayer when standing is excused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the most correct definition of illness that permits a sick person to pray sitting?
A) Falling due to weakness
B) Dizziness of the head
C) Harm from standing
D) Inability to stand at all

Key Takeaway
The sick person must pray sitting only when standing causes harm, and must stand for any portion he is able to.

The Sick Person’s Prayer: Conditions for Sitting and Gesturing
Explains rulings for sick person's prayer when unable to stand, bow, or sit, with an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi views on the definition of sickness that permits sitting in prayer and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
The sick person prays as much as possible; sitting is only permitted if standing causes harm, not mere difficulty.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayra explains the rulings for a sick person who cannot perform the normal postures of prayer. It addresses when standing, bowing, and sitting may be omitted, and highlights an important intra-Hanafi dispute: what exactly constitutes a valid excuse to pray sitting? Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying the school’s position in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Read the lines: 'They differed regarding the definition of the sickness that permits him to pray sitting: It was said: ... and the most correct is ...' List the two definitions.
The first view is based on outward inability (falling); the second on potential harm.
2
Determine the tarjih
Note that the commentator declares the second view 'al-asahh' (the most correct). This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the school.
Tarjih authority: the commentator himself.
3
Analyse the thamarah
Consider a practical scenario: a sick person can stand but feels pain. Under the first view, he is not excused; under the second, he is. Identify the difference in outcome.
The second view is more lenient.
4
Apply the ruling to partial ability
The text also states: 'If he is able to do part of standing... he is ordered to stand as much as he is able.' Apply this rule to a case where the person can stand only for the takbir.
This is consistent with the principle of 'doing what one can'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة المريض
إذا تعذر على المريض القيام : صلَّى قاعداً، يركع ويسجد.
إنما ذَكَرَه عقيب السهو؛ لأن كلا منهما من العوارض، إلا أن السهو أكثر: فكان أهم؛ لأنه يتناول صلاة الصحيح والمريض، فقدمه عليه؛ لشدة مساس الحاجة إلى بيانه.
ثم إضافته : إضافة الفعل إلى فاعله، كـ: قيام زيد.
قال رحمه الله : (إذا تعذر على المريض القيام: صلى قاعداً، يركع ويسجد).
اختلفوا في حد المرض الذي يبيح له الصلاة قاعداً:
فقيل: أن يكون بحال إذا قام سقط من ضعف، أو دوران الرأس، والأصح أن يكون بحيث يلحقه بالقيام ضرر. وإذا كان قادراً على بعض القيام، دون تمامه: أمر بأن يقوم مقدار ما يقدر.
فإذا عجز : قعد، حتى لو قدر أن يكبر قائماً للتحريمة، ولم يقدر على القيام، يعني للقراءة، أو كان يقدر على القيام لبعض القراءة، دون تمامها:
فإنه يُؤمر أن يكبر قائماً، ويقرأ ما يقدر عليه قائماً، ثم يقعد إذا عجز.

وإن قدر عليه متكئاً: لا يجزئه غيره، فيقوم متكئاً.
قوله : صلى قاعداً: يعني: يقعد كيف تيسر عليه.
وإن قدر على القعود مستنداً إلى حائط، أو إلى إنسان: فإنه يجب عليه كذلك، ولا يجزئه مضطجعاً. كذا في «النهاية».
* قوله: (فإن لم يستطع الركوع والسجود : أومأ إيماء) : أومأ: بالهمزة. * قوله: (وجعل السجود أخفض من الركوع)؛ لأن الإيماء قام مقامهما، فأخذ حكمهما.
قوله: (ولا يرفع إلى وجهه شيئاً ليسجد عليه).
فإن رفع : إن وجد الإيماء: جاز، ويكون مسيئاً، وإلا فلا. ولو كان بجبهته قُروحُ لا يستطيع السجود عليها: لم يُجزه الإيماء، وعليه أن يسجد على أنفه، لا يجزئه غير ذلك.
* قوله: (فإن لم يستطع القعودَ : استلقى على ظهره، وجعل رجليه إلى القبلة، يومى للركوع والسجود).
Chapter on the Prayer of the Sick
If standing is impossible for the sick person: he prays sitting, bowing and prostrating.
He [the author] only mentioned it after [the chapter on] forgetfulness because both are from the category of emergencies, except forgetfulness is more common: so it was more important because it applies to the prayer of the healthy and the sick, so he gave it precedence over it due to the strong need for its explanation.
Then its idafah: the idafah of the verb to its doer, like: Zaid's standing.
He [the author], may Allah have mercy on him, said: (If standing is impossible for the sick person: he prays sitting, bowing and prostrating.)
They differed regarding the definition of the sickness that permits him to pray sitting:
It was said: That it be such that if he stands, he falls due to weakness or dizziness; and the most correct is that it be such that standing causes him harm. And if he is able to do part of standing, not all of it: he is ordered to stand as much as he is able.
Then if he becomes unable: he sits, such that even if he is able to say the takbir for the tahrimah standing, but is unable to stand for the recitation, or he is able to stand for some of the recitation, not all of it:
Then he is ordered to say the takbir standing, and recite what he can standing, then sit when he becomes unable.
If he cannot bow and prostrate: he gestures with his head, making the prostration lower than the bowing.
And he does not lift anything to his face to prostrate on.
If he cannot sit: he lies on his back, placing his feet toward the qiblah, and gestures for bowing and prostration.
So his saying: 'If standing is impossible for him' means all of it.
And if he is able to do it while leaning: nothing else suffices him, so he stands leaning.
His saying: 'he prays sitting' means: he sits however it is easy for him.
And if he is able to sit while leaning against a wall or a person: then it is obligatory upon him to do so, and lying on his side does not suffice. Thus in 'al-Nihayah'.
* His saying: (If he cannot bow and prostrate: he gestures with gesture): 'Auma' is with a hamzah. * His saying: (And he makes the prostration lower than the bowing); because the gesture took their place, so it takes their ruling.
His saying: (And he does not lift anything to his face to prostrate on.)
If he does lift: if a gesture is found, it is permissible and he is doing wrong, otherwise not. And if he has ulcers on his forehead that prevent him from prostrating on them: gesture does not suffice him, and he must prostrate on his nose, nothing else suffices.
* His saying: (If he cannot sit: he lies on his back, placing his feet toward the qiblah, and gestures for bowing and prostration.)
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar addresses the prayer of a sick person who cannot perform the normal postures. It begins by noting that the chapter was placed after the chapter on forgetfulness because both deal with exceptions (emergencies) in prayer, but forgetfulness is more common. The core ruling: if standing is impossible, the sick person prays sitting, and if even sitting is impossible, he lies on his back and gestures.

Two views are presented on what constitutes a valid excuse to pray sitting: (1) the person falls from weakness or dizziness upon standing, or (2) the more correct view (al-asahh) is that standing would cause him harm. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute, with the second view being preferred (mu'tamad). Partial ability to stand requires standing for the part one can manage; for example, if one can stand for the opening takbir but not for recitation, one should perform the takbir standing, then sit for the rest.

When bowing and prostration are impossible, the person gestures by lowering the head, with the gesture for prostration being lower than that for bowing. The text emphasizes that one should not lift an object to the forehead to simulate prostration; if one does, it is permissible but disliked (makruh) provided a gesture is made. If the forehead has ulcers, prostration on the nose is required, not gesture.

If sitting is impossible, the person lies on his back with feet toward the qiblah and gestures for bowing and prostration. The commentary also clarifies that if one can stand while leaning on something, that is obligatory. Similarly, if one can sit while leaning against a wall or person, that is obligatory, and lying on the side is not sufficient. These details ensure the prayer is performed as close to the normal form as possible.

Key Terms
تعذر (taʿadhdhura): To become impossible or unfeasible due to excuse; here it means the sick person genuinely cannot perform the posture.
أومأ إيماء (awmaʾa īmāʾan): To gesture with the head (or body) as a substitute for bowing and prostration when one cannot perform them physically.
استلقى (istalqā): To lie on one's back with the face upward and feet toward the qiblah, used when even sitting is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (al-asahh) in the Hanafi school, what condition excuses a sick person from standing in prayer?
A) Inability to stand due to weakness or dizziness.
B) Inability to stand without any assistance.
C) Standing would cause harm to the person.
D) The person is over 60 years old.

Key Takeaway
The sick person must pray as much as possible; sitting is permitted only when standing causes harm, not mere difficulty.

Rulings on Postures for Sick Prayer
Explains rulings for sick person unable to stand, bow, prostrate, or sit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for each posture substitution in sick prayer and apply them to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A sick person must perform what he can of the prayer postures; inability does not remove obligation but substitutes movement with signaling.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the detailed rulings for a sick person who cannot perform the normal postures of prayer. Understanding these rules allows one to correctly apply the principle that the prayer duty remains but the physical movements are replaced by feasible alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the posture and its substitute
For each posture (standing, bowing, prostration, sitting), determine what the sick person must do when unable to perform the normal movement. Note the hierarchy: standing > sitting > lying on back > lying on side.
The passage covers bowing, prostration, sitting, and also standing through the commentary.
2
Distinguish ability with support vs. total inability
If the person can perform a posture with support (leaning on a wall, using a crutch), that support must be used; the concession only applies when even with support the posture is impossible.
The text stresses that ability with support is still ability and does not excuse the posture.
3
Apply the substitution hierarchy sequentially
For each posture, check the degrees: first attempt the original posture; if impossible, move to the next substitute. For example, for standing: sit; for sitting: lie on back; for bowing/prostration: signal with head.
The order is determined by what is closest to the original and what is possible.
4
Handle special cases and exceptions
Note the ruling on lifting an object to prostrate on, the case of forehead sores where prostration on the nose is required, and the note about sleeping invalidating wudu.
These are specific to prostration and to the lying position.
5
Synthesize the full ruling for a given scenario
Given a description of a sick person's limitations, work through the hierarchy step by step and state the required postures and actions.
This ensures the student can operationalize the rules.

فإن صلى مضطجعاً، فنام فيها انتقض وضوؤه. كذا في الوجيز».

Chapter on the prayer of the sick. If he cannot bow and prostrate: he signals with his head, and makes prostration lower than bowing. And he should not lift anything to his face to prostrate on. If he cannot sit: he lies on his back, puts his feet towards the qibla, and signals for bowing and prostration. He said: When standing is difficult for him: it means all of it. And if he is able to do it leaning: nothing else suffices him, so he stands leaning. He said: He prays sitting: it means he sits how it becomes easy for him. And if he is able to sit leaning on a wall or a person: then it is obligatory upon him likewise, and lying down does not suffice him. Thus in al-Nihaya. He said: (If he cannot bow and prostrate: he signals) : 'signals' is with hamza. He said: (and makes prostration lower than bowing); because signaling takes their place, so it takes their ruling. He said: (and he should not lift anything to his face to prostrate on). If he lifts: if signaling is possible, it is permissible and he is at fault, otherwise no. And if he has sores on his forehead and cannot prostrate on them, signaling does not suffice him, and he must prostrate on his nose; nothing else suffices him. He said: (If he cannot sit: he lies on his back, puts his feet towards the qibla, and signals for bowing and prostration). And if he lies on his side, with his face towards the qibla, and signals with his head: it is permissible. If he prays lying down and sleeps in it, his wudu is voided. Thus in al-Wajiz. He said: (and if he lies on his side, with his face towards the qibla, and signals with his head: it is permissible): meaning on his right side, and he puts his head towards the east, but the first is better; if he cannot lie on his right side: then on his left side.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the rules of prayer for a sick person based on the Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It begins by stating the ruling for the sick who cannot bow or prostrate: they should signal with their head, making the gesture for prostration lower than that for bowing, thereby mimicking the sequence. The commentator explains that signaling replaces the actual movements, so the same distinction is observed. Next, the ruling on not lifting an object to the face to prostrate on is given; if one does so and is able to signal, the prayer is valid but disliked, otherwise invalid. An exception is for a person with forehead sores—he must prostrate on his nose, as signaling does not suffice. The text then discusses inability to sit: the sick person lies on his back. The commentary adds that lying on the side with face to qibla is also permissible, preferring the right side. Additionally, the passage clarifies that if a sick person can stand with support, he must stand; similarly, if he can sit with support, he must sit rather than lie down. A note also mentions that if one prays lying down and falls asleep, his wudu is broken. The overall emphasis is that the sick person must perform what he is able to do, with no room for negligence.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-iima'): Signaling with the head as a substitute for bowing and prostration when unable to perform the actual movements
الركوع (al-ruku'): Bowing in prayer, a pillar that is replaced by iima' when unable
السجود (al-sujud): Prostration in prayer, a pillar that is replaced by iima' when unable, with the signal made lower than for ruku'
القعود (al-qu'ud): Sitting posture in prayer; if unable, lying positions are permitted
القيام (al-qiyam): Standing in prayer; if unable, sitting is substituted, but if able with support, standing with support is obligatory

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A sick person who cannot bow or prostrate should:
A) Skip them and continue the prayer.
B) Signal with his head, making the prostration signal lower than the bowing signal.
C) Signal only for bowing, and skip prostration.
D) Place an object on his face to prostrate on.

Key Takeaway
In sick prayer, each posture is substituted with the next feasible alternative, and signaling (iima') replaces bowing and prostration only when actual movement is impossible.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Prayer Obligation When Unable to Signal
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether prayer drops for conscious sick unable to signal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on the obligation of prayer for a conscious sick person who cannot signal with his head, and distinguish the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view from the counter-narration.

Core Idea
A conscious sick person must still pray even if unable to signal for days; consciousness alone suffices for the address.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah addresses a critical intra-madhhab dispute: does the obligation of salah drop when a sick person can no longer even signal with his head? The commentator navigates between two conflicting riwayat within the Hanafi school, one from al-Hidayah and another from Qadi Khan citing Zahir al-Riwayah and Nawadir. Understanding this dispute reveals the hierarchy of authority in Hanafi fiqh and the practical implications for the sick.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the source and note the first position (from the matn and al-Hidayah) that prayer does not drop as long as the person is conscious, even beyond a day and night. The second position, from Qadi Khan citing Zahir al-Riwayah, holds that prayer drops if the inability exceeds a day and night.
The dispute is not about the unconscious (maghma 'alayh) but specifically about the conscious (mufiq) who cannot signal.
2
Examine the evidence for each
The first position argues that the conscious person understands the content of the divine address (yafhamu mazmun al-khitab), so the obligation remains. The second position cites a narration from Muhammad in al-Nawadir: a person with hands cut at elbows and feet at shins has no prayer, implying that mere reason is insufficient.
The hadith from Nawadir is from a weaker transmission source.
3
Analyze the hierarchy of authority
The commentator declares the first view as 'sahih' (correct), relying on al-Hidayah. Although the opposing view is from Zahir al-Riwayah (the core corpus), the commentator weighs the evidence differently. This shows how later scholars (mutakhkhirin) sometimes prefer a position despite its apparent source strength.
Zahir al-Riwayah is normally the most authoritative, but the commentator's tarjih here is based on the strength of the dalil (understanding the address).
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the first view: the conscious sick person delays prayer until able, and must pray when able (even if after days). Under the second view: if the inability exceeds a day and night, no prayer is owed at all.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the first view: obligation does not drop.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله: (فإن لم يستطع الإيماء برأسه : أخر الصلاة).
فيه إشارة إلى أنها لا تسقط إذا بلغ إلى هذه الحالة وإن كان أكثر من يوم وليلة إذا كان مفيقاً، وهو الصحيح؛ لأنه يفهم مضمون الخطاب، بخلاف المغمى عليه. كذا في الهداية ).
قال في «قاضي خان: في ظاهر الرواية: تسقط إذا كان أكثر من يوم وليلة؛ لأن مجرد العقل لا يكفي لتوجه الخطاب؛ لأن محمداً ذكر في «النوادر»: من قطعت يداه من المرفقين، وقدماه من الساقين: لا صلاة عليه، فثبت أن مجرد العقل لا يكفي.
وقيل : إن هذه المسألة على أربعة أوجه:
His statement: (If he cannot signal with his head: he delays the prayer). It contains an indication that it (the prayer) is not dropped when he reaches this state, even if it is more than a day and night as long as he is conscious, and that is the correct opinion; because he understands the content of the address, contrary to the unconscious person. Thus in al-Hidayah. He said in Qadi Khan: In the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), it (the prayer) is dropped if it is more than a day and night; because mere intellect is insufficient for the address to be directed, because Muhammad mentioned in al-Nawadir: 'Whoever has his hands cut from the elbows and his feet from the shins has no prayer upon him,' so it is established that mere intellect is insufficient. And it is said that this issue has four aspects:
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation of prayer for a sick person who cannot even signal with his head. The commentator begins by citing the matn ruling: 'If he cannot signal with his head, he delays the prayer.' The commentary interprets this to mean that the prayer is not dropped entirely as long as the person is conscious (mufiq), even if the inability persists for more than a day and night. This is considered the correct opinion (sahih) because a conscious person can understand the address of obligation, unlike an unconscious person who cannot. This view is supported by al-Hidayah. However, a narration from Qadi Khan cites that in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), the prayer is dropped if the inability exceeds a day and night, because mere consciousness is insufficient for the address to apply. The proof cited is a report from Imam Muhammad in al-Nawadir: a person whose hands are cut at the elbows and feet at the shins has no prayer obligatory on him, indicating that mere intellect does not suffice. The commentator does not explicitly resolve the dispute here but notes that the first view is sahih, indicating the preferred position. The remark that the issue has 'four aspects' suggests further elaboration found in other sources. Overall, the relied-upon Hanafi view for the conscious sick person is that prayer remains obligatory and is delayed, not dropped.

Key Terms
المفيق (al-mufiq): The conscious person who is aware of his surroundings and can understand the address
المغمى عليه (al-mughma 'alayh): The unconscious person who does not perceive; the address of salah is not directed to them
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings from the six books of Imam Muhammad (the core corpus)
النوادر (al-nawadir): Rare transmissions, not from the six core books; weaker authority

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (al-sahih), what is the ruling on a conscious sick person who cannot signal with his head for three days?
A) Prayer drops because of inability.
B) He must still pray when able.
C) He should combine prayers.
D) He is excused after one day.

Key Takeaway
For a conscious sick person unable to signal, the Hanafi mufta bihi is that prayer remains obligatory (fard) and must be delayed and made up later; consciousness, not physical ability, is the key condition.

Sick Person's Prayer: Posture, Nodding, and Wudu Considerations
Ruling for sick person praying while lying on side and nodding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling and conditions for a sick person to pray lying on the side, and identify what nullifies wudu during such prayer.

Core Idea
A sick person may pray lying on his side, facing qibla, nodding for ruku/sujud; sleep during that prayer nullifies wudu.

This lesson covers the Hanafi ruling for a sick person who cannot stand or sit during prayer. The source text from al-Quduri, with commentary from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, explains the permissible posture, the requirement of nodding for movements, and the condition of facing the qibla. It also addresses the nullification of wudu if the person falls asleep, and the preference for lying on the right side.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The sick person may pray while lying on his side and nodding with his head for ruku and sujud. The ruling is ja'iz (permissible).
The matn states: 'wa aumā’a bi-ra’sihī: jāz'.
2
List the conditions for validity
Conditions: lying on the side (preferably right), face towards qibla, nodding for ruku and sujud when unable to perform actual movements. A cushion may be placed under the head to enable nodding.
If unable to nod, prayer is delayed until ability returns.
3
Note the invalidators
If the sick person sleeps during the prayer (even while lying down), wudu is nullified and the prayer becomes invalid.
This is a general rule: sleep nullifies wudu.
4
Understand the preferable side
The right side is preferred when lying down, with head towards the east (qibla direction). If unable, the left side is acceptable.
The commentary clarifies that the right side is first (awla).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة المريض

وإن استلقى على جنبه، ووجهه إلى القبلة، وأومأ برأسه : جاز .

فإن لم يستطع الإيماء برأسه : أخر الصلاة،

يعني بعد أن توضع وسادة تحت رأسه، حتى يتمكن من الإيماء؛ لأن يمنع الإيماء من الأصحاء، فكيف من المرضى!

الاستلقاء

فإن صلى مضطجعاً، فنام فيها انتقض وضوؤه. كذا في الوجيز».

* قوله : (وإن استلقى على جنبه، ووجهه إلى القبلة، وأومأ برأسه : جاز) : يعني على جنبه الأيمن، ويجعل رأسه من قبل المشرق، إلا أن الأول أولى، فإن لم يستطع الاستلقاء على جنبه الأيمن: فعلى جنبه الأيسر.
Chapter on the prayer of the sick. And if he lies down on his side, his face towards the qibla, and he nods with his head: it is permissible. If he is unable to nod with his head: he delays the prayer, meaning after a cushion is placed under his head so that he can nod; because nod is prevented for the healthy, so how about the sick! Lying on side. And if he prays lying down, and then sleeps in it, his wudu is nullified. Thus in al-Wajiz. * His saying: (And if he lies down on his side, his face towards the qibla, and nods with his head: it is permissible) means on his right side, and he makes his head towards the east, except that the first is better; if he cannot lie on his right side: then on his left side.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its commentary from the sharh. It opens with the matn from al-Quduri: the sick person may pray while lying on his side, facing the qibla, and nodding with his head for the actions of bowing and prostration. The commentator explains that a cushion should be placed under his head to facilitate nodding. The ruling also notes that if the sick person sleeps during the prayer, his wudu is broken. The commentary then elaborates that the preferable side is the right side, with the head towards the east (in the direction of the qibla). If unable to lie on the right side, the left side is acceptable. The statement 'delays the prayer' is a separate ruling that will be discussed later. The mention of wudu nullification is a reminder that sleep invalidates wudu, whether in prayer or not, and this is from Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
استلقى (istaqā): Lying down on one’s side, not flat on the back
أومأ برأسه (aumā’a bi-ra’sihī): To indicate the movements of bowing and prostration by nodding the head, instead of actual bowing and prostrating
اضطجع (iḍṭaja'a): To lie down (used for sleeping posture)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a sick person lies on his side, faces the qibla, and nods with his head, what is the ruling?
A) Not permissible
B) Permissible
C) Permissible only if he cannot sit
D) Permissible only for fard prayers

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab allows a sick person to pray while lying on his side (preferably right), facing qibla, and nodding for bowing and prostration, with the note that sleep during the prayer nullifies wudu.

Delaying Prayer for the Bedridden: Does Obligation Drop?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dropping prayer for conscious but immobile sick.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on delayed prayer for a conscious sick person who cannot nod, and evaluate the minority view that the obligation drops after a day and night.

Core Idea
Consciousness sustains obligation; inability to nod delays, not drops, prayer.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses a critical fiqh question: if a sick person cannot even nod with the head, is prayer dropped or merely delayed? The author presents the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar) from al-Hidayah that prayer does not drop as long as the patient is conscious, and then contrasts it with a minority transmission attributed to Qadi Khan from the Nawadir. Understanding this distinction clarifies the Hanafi position on the scope of obligation for those with severe physical incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling from al-Hidayah
The base text says: 'If he is unable to nod with his head: he delays the prayer.' The commentary explains that this indicates prayer does not drop even if the condition lasts more than a day and night, as long as the person is conscious. Note the key condition: consciousness.
The proof is qiyas: the unconscious person is exempt, but the conscious one understands the address.
2
Examine the opposing view from Qadi Khan
Qadi Khan reports that in Zahir al-Riwayah the prayer drops if the illness exceeds a day and night, because mere intellect is insufficient. He cites the hadith-like report from al-Nawadir: 'Whoever has his hands cut off from the elbows and feet from the shins: no prayer upon him.'
Zahir al-Riwayah is the authoritative corpus; however, the report is from al-Nawadir (rarer transmissions).
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The majority (al-Hidayah) hold that consciousness suffices for obligation. The Nawadir report may be specific to those missing limbs entirely, or it may be considered weak against the stronger qiyas-based rule. The dispute hinges on whether mere consciousness is enough for obligation.
The text says 'هو الصحيح' (it is the correct view) from al-Hidayah.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical outcome)
If obligation drops, the sick person does not need to make up any prayers. If it does not drop, he must delay and make up when able (or nod if possible). The mufta bihi is the non-dropping view.
5
Recognise the incomplete four-aspect classification
The text mentions 'this issue has four aspects' but does not detail them here. This signals that the full discussion continues, but the core dispute is already clarified.
In a teaching context, we focus on the established ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله: (فإن لم يستطع الإيماء برأسه : أخر الصلاة).

فيه إشارة إلى أنها لا تسقط إذا بلغ إلى هذه الحالة وإن كان أكثر من يوم وليلة إذا كان مفيقاً، وهو الصحيح؛ لأنه يفهم مضمون الخطاب، بخلاف المغمى عليه. كذا في الهداية ).

قال في «قاضي خان: في ظاهر الرواية: تسقط إذا كان أكثر من يوم وليلة؛ لأن مجرد العقل لا يكفي لتوجه الخطاب؛ لأن محمداً ذكر في «النوادر»: من قطعت يداه من المرفقين، وقدماه من الساقين: لا صلاة عليه، فثبت أن مجرد العقل لا يكفي.

وقيل : إن هذه المسألة على أربعة أوجه:
* His saying: (If he is unable to nod with his head: he delays the prayer). It indicates that (the prayer) is not dropped when he reaches this state even if it is more than a day and a night, if he is conscious – and that is the correct [view]; because he understands the content of the address, unlike the unconscious. Thus in al-Hidayah. He said in Qadi Khan: In Zahir al-Riwayah, it drops if it is more than a day and a night; because mere intellect is insufficient for the address; because Muhammad mentioned in al-Nawadir: Whoever has his hands cut off from the elbows and feet from the shins, there is no prayer upon him. So it is established that mere intellect is not enough. And it is said: This issue has four aspects:
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the obligation of prayer falls from a sick person who cannot even nod with his head. The relied-upon position (al-mukhtar) according to al-Hidayah is that it does not drop as long as the person is conscious, because he can understand the address of obligation. The proof is that an unconscious person is exempted. However, Qadi Khan reports a view in Zahir al-Riwayah (the authoritative corpus) that it drops if the illness lasts more than a day and a night, citing a proof from al-Nawadir: a person without hands and feet is not obligated. The text then indicates the issue has four cases, which are detailed later (on the next page). The dispute is about whether mere consciousness suffices for continued obligation. The Hanafi school follows al-Hidayah's position as the soundest.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zāhir al-Riwāyah): The authoritative corpus of Hanafi fiqh transmitted from Muhammad al-Shaybani in his six books
النوادر (al-Nawādir): Rare or less authoritative transmissions, such as those of Hasan ibn Ziyad
الإيماء (al-īmā'): Nodding or gesturing with the head as a substitute for bowing and prostration in prayer when unable to do full movements.
المغمى عليه (al-mughmā 'alayh): A person who is unconscious; in Hanafi fiqh, his obligation to pray is suspended for the duration of unconsciousness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hidayah (the relied-upon view), what is the ruling for a conscious sick person who cannot nod his head for more than a day and night?
A) The prayer obligation drops entirely.
B) The prayer is delayed and must be made up later.
C) He must nod with his eyebrows.
D) He only prays if he can move other limbs.

Key Takeaway
A conscious sick person who cannot even nod must still intend prayer; the obligation does not drop, but the prayer is delayed until he can perform the movements or make up later.

Nodding with Eyes, Heart, Eyebrows Not Permitted
Ruling that sick person may not nod with eyes, heart, or eyebrows, with opposing views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on permissible nodding for a sick person during prayer and distinguish it from the positions of Zufar, al-Hasan, and al-Shafi‘i.

Core Idea
The sick may only nod with the head; nodding with eyes, heart, or eyebrows invalidates the prayer.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses a critical question: How should a sick person perform the actions of prayer when unable to move their head? The matn gives the strict Hanafi view, followed by three opposing opinions that allow other gestures, each requiring repetition upon recovery. We will analyse the dispute and its practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the explicit prohibition in the matn: he does not nod with eyes, heart, nor eyebrows.
The matn is categorical; no other gestures are permitted.
2
Identify the prohibited gestures
List the body parts explicitly mentioned: eyes, heart, eyebrows. Note that the head is the default permissible part.
Heart nodding means intention or internal movement.
3
Recognize the opposing views
Note Zufar (heart), al-Hasan (eyebrows and heart), and al-Shafi‘i (eyes). All allow some gesture but require repetition (i‘ādah) when the excuse ends.
This shows they consider such prayers valid but deficient.
4
Understand the common requirement of i‘ādah
All three opposing opinions mandate repeating the prayer after recovery, implying the original prayer was not fully valid.
The Hanafi view holds the prayer invalid and must be repeated if done with those gestures.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, a Hanafi who cannot nod must delay prayer; others may pray with gestures but must repeat later.
The key difference: delay vs. perform-and-repeat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يومى بعينيه، ولا بقلبه، ولا بحاجبيه .

وقال زفر: يومي بقلبه، فإذا صح: أعاد.

وقال الحسن: يومي بحاجبيه وقلبه، ويعيد.

وقال الشافعي: يومى بعينيه، فإذا زال العذرُ: أعاد.
And he does not nod with his eyes, nor with his heart, nor with his eyebrows. Zufar said: He nods with his heart, and when he recovers, he repeats. Al-Hasan said: He nods with his eyebrows and heart, and repeats. Al-Shafi’i said: He nods with his eyes, and when the excuse ceases, he repeats.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on how a sick person may indicate the actions of prayer when unable to nod with the head. The matn states clearly that nodding with the eyes, heart, or eyebrows is not acceptable. The commentary then reports the views of three scholars who allow other forms of nodding but require repetition upon recovery. Zufar (a Hanafi) allows heart nodding but requires iʿādah. Al-Hasan (likely al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, a Nawadir transmitter) allows eyebrows and heart, also with iʿādah. Al-Shafiʿi allows eye nodding, also with iʿādah. The Hanafi position (the matn) is that these are invalid methods; prayer must be delayed if head nodding is impossible. The reason is that the body parts involved (eyes, heart, eyebrows) do not represent the actions of ruku and sujud in a meaningful way. This ruling prevents the prayer from being performed in a way that deviates from the established norms.

Key Terms
أومأ (aumā’a): To indicate the actions of prayer (ruku and sujud) by head movement
يعيد (yu‘īdu): To repeat the prayer (when the excuse ceases) because the previous prayer was invalid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn in this passage, with which body part may a sick person nod for prayer?
A) Eyes
B) Heart
C) Head
D) Eyebrows

Key Takeaway
A sick person may only nod with the head; any other gesture invalidates the prayer and requires repetition upon recovery.
